</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2">Signature</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Friedrich_Nietzsche_Signature.svg"><img alt="Friedrich Nietzsche Signature.svg" data-file-height="66" data-file-width="262" decoding="async" height="38" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Friedrich_Nietzsche_Signature.svg/150px-Friedrich_Nietzsche_Signature.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Friedrich_Nietzsche_Signature.svg/225px-Friedrich_Nietzsche_Signature.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Friedrich_Nietzsche_Signature.svg/300px-Friedrich_Nietzsche_Signature.svg.png 2x" width="150"/></a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Friedrich Wilhelm Nietzsche</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/iË/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iË</span><span title="/tÊ/: 'ch' in 'China'">tÊ</span><span title="/É/: 'a' in 'about'">É</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/tÊ/: 'ch' in 'China'">tÊ</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> <small>German: </small><span class="IPA" lang="de-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ËfÊiËdÊÉªÃ§ ËvÉªlhÉlm ËniËtÊÉ]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:De-Friedrich_Nietzsche.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fc/De-Friedrich_Nietzsche.ogg" title="De-Friedrich Nietzsche.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span> <small>or</small> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="de-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ËniËtsÊÉ]</a></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> 15 October 1844 â 25 August 1900) was a German <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cultural_critic" title="Cultural critic">cultural critic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philologist</a> whose work has exerted a profound influence on <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_philosophy" title="Contemporary philosophy">modern intellectual history</a>. He began his career as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_philology" title="Classical philology">classical philologist</a> before turning to <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>. He became the youngest person ever to hold the Chair of Classical Philology at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Basel" title="University of Basel">University of Basel</a> in 1869 at the age of 24. Nietzsche resigned in 1879 due to health problems that plagued him most of his life; he completed much of his core writing in the following decade. In 1889, at age 45, he suffered a collapse and afterward a complete loss of his mental faculties, with <a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">paralysis</a> and probably <a href="/wiki/Vascular_dementia" title="Vascular dementia">vascular dementia</a>. He lived his remaining years in the care of his mother until her death in 1897 and then with his sister <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_F%C3%B6rster-Nietzsche" title="Elisabeth FÃ¶rster-Nietzsche">Elisabeth FÃ¶rster-Nietzsche</a>. Nietzsche died in 1900, after many <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">strokes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Friedrich_Nietzsche_Portraite.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4218" data-file-width="2983" decoding="async" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Friedrich_Nietzsche_Portraite.jpg/220px-Friedrich_Nietzsche_Portraite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Friedrich_Nietzsche_Portraite.jpg/330px-Friedrich_Nietzsche_Portraite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Friedrich_Nietzsche_Portraite.jpg/440px-Friedrich_Nietzsche_Portraite.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Friedrich_Nietzsche_Portraite.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Portrait" title="Portrait">Portrait</a> of Friedrich Nietzsche</div></div></div>
<p>Nietzsche's writing spans <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophical</a> <a href="/wiki/Polemic" title="Polemic">polemics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">poetry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cultural_critic" title="Cultural critic">cultural criticism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fiction" title="Fiction">fiction</a> while displaying a fondness for <a href="/wiki/Aphorism" title="Aphorism">aphorism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irony" title="Irony">irony</a>. Prominent elements of his philosophy include his radical critique of <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> in favor of <a href="/wiki/Perspectivism" title="Perspectivism">perspectivism</a>; a <a href="/wiki/Genealogy_(philosophy)" title="Genealogy (philosophy)">genealogical</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critique_of_religion" title="Critique of religion">critique of religion</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_morality" title="Christian morality">Christian morality</a> and a related theory of <a href="/wiki/Master%E2%80%93slave_morality" title="Masterâslave morality">masterâslave morality</a>; the aesthetic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Affirmation_of_life" title="Affirmation of life">affirmation of life</a> in response to both the "<a href="/wiki/God_is_dead" title="God is dead">death of God</a>" and the profound crisis of <a href="/wiki/Nihilism" title="Nihilism">nihilism</a>; the notion of <a href="/wiki/Apollonian_and_Dionysian" title="Apollonian and Dionysian">Apollonian and Dionysian</a> forces; and a characterization of the human <a href="/wiki/Subject_(philosophy)" title="Subject (philosophy)">subject</a> as the expression of competing <a href="/wiki/Will_(philosophy)" title="Will (philosophy)">wills</a>, collectively understood as the <a href="/wiki/Will_to_power" title="Will to power">will to power</a>. He also developed influential concepts such as the <i><span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/%C3%9Cbermensch" title="Ãbermensch">Ãbermensch</a></i></span></i> and his doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Eternal_return#Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Eternal return">eternal return</a>. In his later work, he became increasingly preoccupied with the creative powers of the individual to overcome cultural and moral mores in pursuit of <a href="/wiki/Transvaluation_of_values" title="Transvaluation of values">new values</a> and aesthetic health. His body of work touched a wide range of topics, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_Art" title="Philosophy of Art">art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_history" title="Philosophy of history">history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_music" title="Philosophy of music">music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_religion" title="Philosophy of religion">religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tragedy" title="Tragedy">tragedy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_culture" title="Philosophy of culture">culture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science" title="Philosophy of science">science</a>, and drew inspiration from <a href="/wiki/Greek_tragedy" title="Greek tragedy">Greek tragedy</a> as well as figures such as <a href="/wiki/Zoroaster" title="Zoroaster">Zoroaster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Arthur Schopenhauer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson">Ralph Waldo Emerson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a>.
</p><p>After his death, his sister Elisabeth became the curator and editor of Nietzsche's manuscripts. She edited his unpublished writings to fit her <a href="/wiki/German_nationalism#1871_to_World_War_I,_1914â1918" title="German nationalism">German ultranationalist</a> ideology while often contradicting or obfuscating Nietzsche's stated opinions, which were explicitly <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_Friedrich_Nietzsche#Criticism_of_Anti-Semitism_and_nationalism" title="Philosophy of Friedrich Nietzsche">opposed to antisemitism and nationalism</a>. Through her published editions, Nietzsche's work became associated with <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">fascism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a>; 20th-century scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Walter_Kaufmann_(philosopher)" title="Walter Kaufmann (philosopher)">Walter Kaufmann</a>, <a href="/wiki/R._J._Hollingdale" title="R. J. Hollingdale">R. J. Hollingdale</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Georges_Bataille" title="Georges Bataille">Georges Bataille</a> defended Nietzsche against this interpretation, and corrected editions of his writings were soon made available. Nietzsche's thought enjoyed renewed popularity in the 1960s and his ideas have since had a profound impact on 20th- and early 21st-century thinkers across philosophyâespecially in schools of <a href="/wiki/Continental_philosophy" title="Continental philosophy">continental philosophy</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Existentialism" title="Existentialism">existentialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Postmodernism" title="Postmodernism">postmodernism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Post-structuralism" title="Post-structuralism">post-structuralism</a>âas well as art, literature, <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">poetry</a>, politics, and popular culture.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Youth_.281844.E2.80.931868.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Youth_(1844â1868)">Youth (1844â1868)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Youth (1844â1868)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span class="anchor" id="youth"></span>
</p><p>Born on 15 October 1844, Nietzsche grew up in the town of <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B6cken" title="RÃ¶cken">RÃ¶cken</a> (now part of <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCtzen" title="LÃ¼tzen">LÃ¼tzen</a>), near <a href="/wiki/Leipzig" title="Leipzig">Leipzig</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Prussian</a> <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Saxony" title="Province of Saxony">Province of Saxony</a>. He was named after King <a href="/wiki/Frederick_William_IV_of_Prussia" title="Frederick William IV of Prussia">Friedrich Wilhelm IV of Prussia</a>, who turned 49 on the day of Nietzsche's birth (Nietzsche later dropped his middle name Wilhelm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaufmann197422_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaufmann197422-13">[13]</a></sup> Nietzsche's parents, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Ludwig_Nietzsche" title="Carl Ludwig Nietzsche">Carl Ludwig Nietzsche</a> (1813â1849), a <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> <a href="/wiki/Pastor" title="Pastor">pastor</a> and former teacher; and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Franziska_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Franziska Nietzsche (page does not exist)">Franziska Nietzsche</a> (<i>nÃ©e</i> Oehler) (1826â1897), married in 1843, the year before their son's birth. They had two other children: a daughter, <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_F%C3%B6rster-Nietzsche" title="Elisabeth FÃ¶rster-Nietzsche">Elisabeth FÃ¶rster-Nietzsche</a>, born in 1846; and a second son, Ludwig Joseph, born in 1848. Nietzsche's father died from a brain ailment in 1849; Ludwig Joseph died six months later at age two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wicks_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wicks-14">[14]</a></sup> The family then moved to <a href="/wiki/Naumburg" title="Naumburg">Naumburg</a>, where they lived with Nietzsche's maternal grandmother and his father's two unmarried sisters. After the death of Nietzsche's grandmother in 1856, the family moved into their own house, now <a href="/wiki/Nietzsche-Haus,_Naumburg" title="Nietzsche-Haus, Naumburg">Nietzsche-Haus</a>, a museum and Nietzsche study center.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche1861.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="472" data-file-width="291" decoding="async" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Nietzsche1861.jpg/170px-Nietzsche1861.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Nietzsche1861.jpg/255px-Nietzsche1861.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7f/Nietzsche1861.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche1861.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young Nietzsche, 1861</div></div></div>
<p>Nietzsche attended a boys' school and then a private school, where he became friends with Gustav Krug and Wilhelm Pinder, all three of whom came from highly respected families. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academic_Record" title="Academic Record">Academic records</a> from one of the schools attended by Nietzsche noted that he excelled in <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">Christian theology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human,_All_Too_Human,_BBC_Documentary,_1999_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Human,_All_Too_Human,_BBC_Documentary,_1999-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="may be unavailable and/or a breach of BBC copyright (October 2019)">betterÂ sourceÂ needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In 1854, he began to attend Domgymnasium in Naumburg. Because his father had worked for the state (as a pastor) the now-fatherless Nietzsche was offered a <a href="/wiki/Scholarship" title="Scholarship">scholarship</a> to study at the internationally recognized <a href="/wiki/Pforta" title="Pforta">Schulpforta</a> (the claim that Nietzsche was admitted on the strength of his academic competence has been debunked: his grades were not near the top of the class).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> He studied there from 1858 to 1864, becoming friends with <a href="/wiki/Paul_Deussen" title="Paul Deussen">Paul Deussen</a> and Carl von Gersdorff. He also found time to work on poems and musical compositions. Nietzsche led "Germania", a music and literature club, during his summers in Naumburg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wicks_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wicks-14">[14]</a></sup> At Schulpforta, Nietzsche received an important grounding in languagesâ<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>, and <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>âso as to be able to read important <a href="/wiki/Primary_source" title="Primary source">primary sources</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> he also experienced for the first time being away from his family life in a small-town conservative environment. His end-of-semester exams in March 1864 showed a 1<span class="nowrap">Â </span>in Religion and German; a 2a in Greek and Latin; a 2b in French, History, and Physics; and a "lackluster" 3<span class="nowrap">Â </span>in Hebrew and Mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECate200537_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECate200537-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nietzsche was an amateur composer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grove_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grove-19">[19]</a></sup> He composed several works for voice, piano, and violin beginning in 1858 at the Schulpforta in Naumburg when he started to work on musical compositions. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a> was dismissive of Nietzsche's music, allegedly mocking a birthday gift of a piano composition sent by Nietzsche in 1871 to his wife <a href="/wiki/Cosima_Wagner" title="Cosima Wagner">Cosima</a>. German conductor and pianist <a href="/wiki/Hans_von_B%C3%BClow" title="Hans von BÃ¼low">Hans von BÃ¼low</a> also described another of Nietzsche's pieces as "the most undelightful and the most antimusical draft on musical paper that I have faced in a long time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>While at Schulpforta, Nietzsche pursued subjects that were considered unbecoming. He became acquainted with the work of the then almost-unknown poet <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_H%C3%B6lderlin" title="Friedrich HÃ¶lderlin">Friedrich HÃ¶lderlin</a>, calling him "my favorite poet" and writing an <a href="/wiki/Essay" title="Essay">essay</a> in which he said that the poet raised consciousness to "the most sublime ideality".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayman198042_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayman198042-21">[21]</a></sup> The teacher who corrected the essay gave it a good mark but commented that Nietzsche should concern himself in the future with healthier, more lucid, and more "German" writers. Additionally, he became acquainted with <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Ortlepp" title="Ernst Ortlepp">Ernst Ortlepp</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Eccentricity_(behavior)" title="Eccentricity (behavior)">eccentric</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blasphemous" title="Blasphemous">blasphemous</a>, and often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drunken" title="Drunken">drunken</a> poet who was found dead in a ditch weeks after meeting the young Nietzsche but who may have introduced Nietzsche to the music and writing of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Perhaps under Ortlepp's influence, he and a student named Richter returned to school drunk and encountered a teacher, resulting in Nietzsche's demotion from first in his class and the end of his status as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/School_prefect" title="School prefect">prefect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHollingdale199921_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHollingdale199921-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche-21.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="294" data-file-width="220" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Nietzsche-21.jpg/170px-Nietzsche-21.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cd/Nietzsche-21.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche-21.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young Nietzsche</div></div></div>
<p>After graduation in September 1864,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Nietzsche began studying theology and classical philology at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bonn" title="University of Bonn">University of Bonn</a> in the hope of becoming a <a href="/wiki/Minister_(Christianity)" title="Minister (Christianity)">minister</a>. For a short time, he and Deussen became members of the <a href="/wiki/Burschenschaft" title="Burschenschaft">Burschenschaft</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Franconia" title="Franconia">Frankonia</a></i>. After one semester (and to the anger of his mother), he stopped his theological studies and lost his faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaberg_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schaberg-25">[25]</a></sup> As early as his 1862 essay "Fate and History", Nietzsche had argued that historical research had discredited the central teachings of Christianity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> but <a href="/wiki/David_Strauss" title="David Strauss">David Strauss</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/David_Strauss#Das_Leben_Jesu" title="David Strauss">Life of Jesus</a></i> also seems to have had a profound effect on the young man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaberg_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schaberg-25">[25]</a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Feuerbach" title="Ludwig Feuerbach">Ludwig Feuerbach</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Essence_of_Christianity" title="The Essence of Christianity">The Essence of Christianity</a></i> influenced young Nietzsche with its argument that people created God, and not the other way around.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Higgins_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Higgins-27">[27]</a></sup> In June 1865, at the age of 20, Nietzsche wrote to his sister Elisabeth, who was deeply religious, a letter regarding his loss of faith. This letter contains the following statement:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Hence the ways of men part: if you wish to strive for peace of soul and pleasure, then believe; if you wish to be a devotee of truth, then inquire....<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frankfurt_Am_Main-Portraits-Arthur_Schopenhauer-1845.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="964" data-file-width="736" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Frankfurt_Am_Main-Portraits-Arthur_Schopenhauer-1845.jpg/170px-Frankfurt_Am_Main-Portraits-Arthur_Schopenhauer-1845.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Frankfurt_Am_Main-Portraits-Arthur_Schopenhauer-1845.jpg/255px-Frankfurt_Am_Main-Portraits-Arthur_Schopenhauer-1845.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Frankfurt_Am_Main-Portraits-Arthur_Schopenhauer-1845.jpg/340px-Frankfurt_Am_Main-Portraits-Arthur_Schopenhauer-1845.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frankfurt_Am_Main-Portraits-Arthur_Schopenhauer-1845.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Arthur Schopenhauer</a> strongly influenced Nietzsche's philosophical thought.</div></div></div>
<p>Nietzsche subsequently concentrated on studying philology under Professor <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_Ritschl" title="Friedrich Wilhelm Ritschl">Friedrich Wilhelm Ritschl</a>, whom he followed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Leipzig" title="University of Leipzig">University of Leipzig</a> in 1865.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagnus1999_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagnus1999-29">[29]</a></sup> There he became close friends with his fellow student <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Rohde" title="Erwin Rohde">Erwin Rohde</a>. Nietzsche's first philological publications appeared soon after.
</p><p>In 1865, Nietzsche thoroughly studied the works of <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Arthur Schopenhauer</a>. He owed the awakening of his philosophical interest to reading Schopenhauer's <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_as_Will_and_Representation" title="The World as Will and Representation">The World as Will and Representation</a></i> and later admitted that Schopenhauer was one of the few thinkers whom he respected, dedicating the essay "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Schopenhauer_as_Educator" title="s:Schopenhauer as Educator">Schopenhauer as Educator</a>" in the <i><a href="/wiki/Untimely_Meditations" title="Untimely Meditations">Untimely Meditations</a></i> to him.
</p><p>In 1866, he read <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Albert_Lange" title="Friedrich Albert Lange">Friedrich Albert Lange</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Materialism_and_Critique_of_its_Present_Importance" title="History of Materialism and Critique of its Present Importance">History of Materialism</a></i>. Lange's descriptions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kant" title="Kant">Kant</a>'s anti-materialistic philosophy, the rise of European <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">Materialism</a>, Europe's increased concern with science, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>'s theory of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>, and the general rebellion against tradition and authority intrigued Nietzsche greatly. Nietzsche would ultimately argue the impossibility of an evolutionary explanation of the human aesthetic sense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1867, Nietzsche signed up for one year of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enlistment" title="Enlistment">voluntary service</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Prussian_Army" title="Prussian Army">Prussian artillery division</a> in Naumburg. He was regarded as one of the finest <a href="/wiki/Horses_in_warfare" title="Horses in warfare">riders</a> among his fellow recruits, and his officers predicted that he would soon reach the rank of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Captain_(Army)" title="Captain (Army)">captain</a>. However, in March 1868, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horseback_riding" title="Horseback riding">jumping into the saddle</a> of his horse, Nietzsche struck his chest against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pommel_(saddle)" title="Pommel (saddle)">pommel</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle_tear" title="Muscle tear">tore two muscles</a> in his left side, leaving him exhausted and unable to walk for months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayman198093_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayman198093-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Consequently, he turned his attention to his studies again, completing them in 1868. Nietzsche also met <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a> for the first time later that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Professor_at_Basel_.281869.E2.80.931879.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Professor_at_Basel_(1869â1879)">Professor at Basel (1869â1879)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Professor at Basel (1869â1879)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Professor_at_Basel"></span><span class="anchor" id="Basel"></span>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:11-11-24-basel-by-ralfr-035.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4288" data-file-width="2848" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/11-11-24-basel-by-ralfr-035.jpg/170px-11-11-24-basel-by-ralfr-035.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/11-11-24-basel-by-ralfr-035.jpg/255px-11-11-24-basel-by-ralfr-035.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/11-11-24-basel-by-ralfr-035.jpg/340px-11-11-24-basel-by-ralfr-035.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:11-11-24-basel-by-ralfr-035.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Basel" title="University of Basel">University of Basel</a>, where Friedrich Nietzsche became a professor in 1869</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rohde_Gersdorff_Nietzsche.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1438" data-file-width="843" decoding="async" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Rohde_Gersdorff_Nietzsche.JPG/170px-Rohde_Gersdorff_Nietzsche.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Rohde_Gersdorff_Nietzsche.JPG/255px-Rohde_Gersdorff_Nietzsche.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Rohde_Gersdorff_Nietzsche.JPG/340px-Rohde_Gersdorff_Nietzsche.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rohde_Gersdorff_Nietzsche.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Left to right: <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Rohde" title="Erwin Rohde">Erwin Rohde</a>, Karl von Gersdorff and Nietzsche, October 1871</div></div></div>
<p>In 1869, with Ritschl's support, Nietzsche received an offer to become a professor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_philology" title="Classical philology">classical philology</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Basel" title="University of Basel">University of Basel</a> in Switzerland. He was only 24 years old and had neither completed his doctorate nor received a teaching certificate ("<a href="/wiki/Habilitation" title="Habilitation">habilitation</a>"). He was awarded an honorary doctorate by <a href="/wiki/Leipzig_University" title="Leipzig University">Leipzig University</a> in March 1869, again with Ritschl's support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJensenHeit2014129_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJensenHeit2014129-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite his offer coming at a time when he was considering giving up philology for science, he accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaufmann197425_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaufmann197425-35">[35]</a></sup> To this day, Nietzsche is still among the youngest of the tenured Classics professors on record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nietzsche's 1870 projected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctoral_thesis" title="Doctoral thesis">doctoral thesis</a>, "Contribution toward the Study and the Critique of the Sources of Diogenes Laertius" ("BeitrÃ¤ge zur Quellenkunde und Kritik des Laertius Diogenes"), examined the origins of the ideas of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diogenes_La%C3%ABrtius" title="Diogenes LaÃ«rtius">Diogenes LaÃ«rtius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJensenHeit2014115_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJensenHeit2014115-37">[37]</a></sup> Though never submitted, it was later published as a <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Gratulationsschrift</i></span> ('congratulatory publication') in <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[i]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before moving to Basel, Nietzsche renounced his Prussian citizenship: for the rest of his life he remained officially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stateless_person" title="Stateless person">stateless</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nevertheless, Nietzsche served in the Prussian forces during the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Prussian_War" title="Franco-Prussian War">Franco-Prussian War</a> (1870â1871) as a medical <a href="/wiki/Orderly" title="Orderly">orderly</a>. In his short time in the military, he experienced much and witnessed the traumatic effects of battle. He also contracted <a href="/wiki/Diphtheria" title="Diphtheria">diphtheria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dysentery" title="Dysentery">dysentery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-42">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Walter_Kaufmann_(philosopher)" title="Walter Kaufmann (philosopher)">Walter Kaufmann</a> speculates that he might also have contracted <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a> at a brothel along with his other infections at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> On returning to Basel in 1870, Nietzsche observed the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a>'s subsequent policies as an outsider and with a degree of skepticism regarding their genuineness. His inaugural lecture at the university was "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Homer_and_Classical_Philology" title="s:Homer and Classical Philology">Homer and Classical Philology</a>". Nietzsche also met <a href="/wiki/Franz_Overbeck" title="Franz Overbeck">Franz Overbeck</a>, a professor of theology who remained his friend throughout his life. <a href="/wiki/Afrikan_Spir" title="Afrikan Spir">Afrikan Spir</a>, a little-known Russian philosopher responsible for the 1873 <i>Thought and Reality</i> and Nietzsche's colleague, the famed historian <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Burckhardt" title="Jacob Burckhardt">Jacob Burckhardt</a>, whose lectures Nietzsche frequently attended, began to exercise significant influence on him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nietzsche had already met <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a> in Leipzig in 1868 and later Wagner's wife, <a href="/wiki/Cosima_Wagner" title="Cosima Wagner">Cosima</a>. Nietzsche admired both greatly and during his time at Basel frequently visited Wagner's house in <a href="/wiki/Tribschen" title="Tribschen">Tribschen</a> in <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Lucerne" title="Canton of Lucerne">Lucerne</a>. The Wagners brought Nietzsche into their most intimate circleâincluding <a href="/wiki/Franz_Liszt" title="Franz Liszt">Franz Liszt</a>, of whom Nietzsche colloquially described: "Liszt or the art of running after women!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> Nietzsche enjoyed the attention he gave to the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Bayreuth_Festspielhaus" title="Bayreuth Festspielhaus">Bayreuth Festival</a>. In 1870, he gave Cosima Wagner the manuscript of "The Genesis of the Tragic Idea" as a birthday gift. In 1872, Nietzsche published his first book, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Birth_of_Tragedy" title="The Birth of Tragedy">The Birth of Tragedy</a></i>. However, his colleagues within his field, including Ritschl, expressed little enthusiasm for the work in which Nietzsche eschewed the classical philologic method in favor of a more speculative approach. In his <a href="/wiki/Polemic" title="Polemic">polemic</a> <i>Philology of the Future</i>, <a href="/wiki/Ulrich_von_Wilamowitz-Moellendorff" title="Ulrich von Wilamowitz-Moellendorff">Ulrich von Wilamowitz-Moellendorff</a> damped the book's reception and increased its notoriety. In response, Rohde (then a professor in <a href="/wiki/Kiel" title="Kiel">Kiel</a>) and Wagner came to Nietzsche's defense. Nietzsche remarked freely about the isolation he felt within the philological community and attempted unsuccessfully to transfer to a position in philosophy at Basel.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Friedrich_Nietzsche-1872.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="531" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Friedrich_Nietzsche-1872.jpg/170px-Friedrich_Nietzsche-1872.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Friedrich_Nietzsche-1872.jpg/255px-Friedrich_Nietzsche-1872.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Friedrich_Nietzsche-1872.jpg/340px-Friedrich_Nietzsche-1872.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Friedrich_Nietzsche-1872.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nietzsche, c. 1872</div></div></div>
<p>In 1873, Nietzsche began to accumulate notes that would be posthumously published as <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophy_in_the_Tragic_Age_of_the_Greeks" title="Philosophy in the Tragic Age of the Greeks">Philosophy in the Tragic Age of the Greeks</a></i>. Between 1873 and 1876, he published four separate long essays: "<a href="/wiki/David_Strauss" title="David Strauss">David Strauss</a>: the Confessor and the Writer", "On the Use and Abuse of History for Life", "Schopenhauer as Educator", and "Richard Wagner in Bayreuth". These four later appeared in a collected edition under the title <i><a href="/wiki/Untimely_Meditations" title="Untimely Meditations">Untimely Meditations</a></i>. The essays shared the orientation of a cultural critique, challenging the developing German culture suggested by Schopenhauer and Wagner. During this time in the circle of the Wagners, he met <a href="/wiki/Malwida_von_Meysenbug" title="Malwida von Meysenbug">Malwida von Meysenbug</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hans_von_B%C3%BClow" title="Hans von BÃ¼low">Hans von BÃ¼low</a>. He also began a friendship with <a href="/wiki/Paul_R%C3%A9e" title="Paul RÃ©e">Paul RÃ©e</a> who, in 1876, influenced him into dismissing the pessimism in his early writings. However, he was deeply disappointed by the <a href="/wiki/Bayreuth_Festival" title="Bayreuth Festival">Bayreuth Festival</a> of 1876, where the banality of the shows and baseness of the public repelled him. He was also alienated by Wagner's championing of "German culture", which Nietzsche felt a contradiction in terms as well as by Wagner's celebration of his fame among the German public. All this contributed to his subsequent decision to distance himself from Wagner.
</p><p>With the publication in 1878 of <i><a href="/wiki/Human,_All_Too_Human" title="Human, All Too Human">Human, All Too Human</a></i> (a book of <a href="/wiki/Aphorism" title="Aphorism">aphorisms</a> ranging from metaphysics to morality to religion), a new style of Nietzsche's work became clear, highly influenced by <a href="/wiki/Afrikan_Spir" title="Afrikan Spir">Afrikan Spir</a>'s <i>Thought and Reality</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> and reacting against the pessimistic philosophy of Wagner and Schopenhauer. Nietzsche's friendship with Deussen and Rohde cooled as well. In 1879, after a significant decline in health, Nietzsche had to resign his position at Basel. Since his childhood, various disruptive illnesses had plagued him, including moments of shortsightedness that left him nearly blind, <a href="/wiki/Migraine" title="Migraine">migraine</a> headaches, and violent indigestion. The 1868 riding accident and diseases in 1870 may have aggravated these persistent conditions, which continued to affect him through his years at Basel, forcing him to take longer and longer holidays until regular work became impractical.
</p>
<h3><span id="Independent_philosopher_.281879.E2.80.931888.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Independent_philosopher_(1879â1888)">Independent philosopher (1879â1888)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Independent philosopher (1879â1888)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span class="anchor" id="independent_philosopher"></span><span class="anchor" id="philosopher"></span>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche_paul-ree_lou-von-salome188.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="661" data-file-width="442" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Nietzsche_paul-ree_lou-von-salome188.jpg/170px-Nietzsche_paul-ree_lou-von-salome188.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Nietzsche_paul-ree_lou-von-salome188.jpg/255px-Nietzsche_paul-ree_lou-von-salome188.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Nietzsche_paul-ree_lou-von-salome188.jpg/340px-Nietzsche_paul-ree_lou-von-salome188.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche_paul-ree_lou-von-salome188.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lou_Andreas-Salom%C3%A9" title="Lou Andreas-SalomÃ©">Lou SalomÃ©</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_R%C3%A9e" title="Paul RÃ©e">Paul RÃ©e</a> and Nietzsche traveled through Italy in 1882, planning to establish an educational commune together, but the friendship disintegrated in late 1882 due to complications from RÃ©e's and Nietzsche's mutual romantic interest in Lou Andreas-SalomÃ©.</div></div></div>
<p>Living off his pension from Basel and aid from friends, Nietzsche traveled frequently to find climates more conducive to his health and lived until 1889 as an independent author in different cities. He spent many summers in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sils_Maria" title="Sils Maria">Sils Maria</a> near <a href="/wiki/St._Moritz" title="St. Moritz">St. Moritz</a> in Switzerland. He spent his winters in the Italian cities of <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rapallo" title="Rapallo">Rapallo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">Turin</a> and the French city of <a href="/wiki/Nice" title="Nice">Nice</a>. In 1881, when <a href="/wiki/French_conquest_of_Tunisia" title="French conquest of Tunisia">France occupied Tunisia</a>, he planned to travel to <a href="/wiki/Tunis" title="Tunis">Tunis</a> to view Europe from the outside but later abandoned that idea, probably for health reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> Nietzsche occasionally returned to Naumburg to visit his family, and, especially during this time, he and <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_F%C3%B6rster-Nietzsche" title="Elisabeth FÃ¶rster-Nietzsche">his sister</a> had repeated periods of conflict and reconciliation.
</p><p>While in <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a>, Nietzsche's failing eyesight prompted him to explore the use of <a href="/wiki/Typewriter" title="Typewriter">typewriters</a> as a means of continuing to write. He is known to have tried using the <a href="/wiki/Hansen_Writing_Ball#Sale_and_popular_use" title="Hansen Writing Ball">Hansen Writing Ball</a>, a contemporary typewriter device. In the end, a past student of his, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_K%C3%B6selitz" title="Heinrich KÃ¶selitz">Peter Gast</a>, became a private secretary to Nietzsche. In 1876, Gast transcribed the crabbed, nearly illegible handwriting of Nietzsche's first time with Richard Wagner in Bayreuth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECate2005221_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECate2005221-49">[48]</a></sup> He subsequently transcribed and proofread the galleys for almost all of Nietzsche's work. On at least one occasion, on 23 February 1880, the usually poor Gast received 200 marks from their mutual friend, Paul RÃ©e.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECate2005297_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECate2005297-50">[49]</a></sup> Gast was one of the very few friends Nietzsche allowed to criticize him. In responding most enthusiastically to <i><a href="/wiki/Thus_Spoke_Zarathustra" title="Thus Spoke Zarathustra">Also Sprach Zarathustra</a></i> ("Thus Spoke Zarathustra"), Gast did feel it necessary to point out that what were described as "superfluous" people were in fact quite necessary. He went on to list the number of people <a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a>, for example, had to rely on to supply his simple diet of goat cheese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECate2005415_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECate2005415-51">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>To the end of his life, Gast and Overbeck remained consistently faithful friends. <a href="/wiki/Malwida_von_Meysenbug" title="Malwida von Meysenbug">Malwida von Meysenbug</a> remained like a motherly patron even outside the Wagner circle. Soon Nietzsche made contact with the music-critic Carl Fuchs. Nietzsche stood at the beginning of his most productive period. Beginning with <i><a href="/wiki/Human,_All_Too_Human" title="Human, All Too Human">Human, All Too Human</a></i> in 1878, Nietzsche published one book or major section of a book each year until 1888, his last year of writing; that year, he completed five.
</p><p>In 1882, Nietzsche published the first part of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gay_Science" title="The Gay Science">The Gay Science</a></i>. That year he also met <a href="/wiki/Lou_Andreas-Salom%C3%A9" title="Lou Andreas-SalomÃ©">Lou Andreas-SalomÃ©</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> through Malwida von Meysenbug and <a href="/wiki/Paul_R%C3%A9e" title="Paul RÃ©e">Paul RÃ©e</a>.
</p><p>SalomÃ©'s mother took her to Rome when SalomÃ© was 21. At a literary salon in the city, SalomÃ© became acquainted with <a href="/wiki/Paul_R%C3%A9e" title="Paul RÃ©e">Paul RÃ©e</a>. RÃ©e proposed marriage to her, but she, instead, proposed that they should live and study together as "brother and sister", along with another man for company, where they would establish an academic commune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHollingdale1999149_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHollingdale1999149-53">[52]</a></sup> RÃ©e accepted the idea and suggested that they be joined by his friend Nietzsche. The two met Nietzsche in Rome in April 1882, and Nietzsche is believed to have instantly fallen in love with SalomÃ©, as RÃ©e had done. Nietzsche asked RÃ©e to propose marriage to SalomÃ©, which she rejected. She had been interested in Nietzsche as a friend, but not as a husband.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHollingdale1999149_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHollingdale1999149-53">[52]</a></sup> Nietzsche nonetheless was content to join with RÃ©e and SalomÃ© touring through Switzerland and Italy together, planning their commune. The three traveled with SalomÃ©'s mother through Italy and considered where they would set up their "Winterplan" commune. They intended to set up their commune in an abandoned monastery, but no suitable location was found. On 13 May, in Lucerne, when Nietzsche was alone with SalomÃ©, he earnestly proposed marriage to her again, which she rejected. He nonetheless was happy to continue with the plans for an academic commune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHollingdale1999149_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHollingdale1999149-53">[52]</a></sup> After discovering the relationship, Nietzsche's sister Elisabeth became determined to get Nietzsche away from the "immoral woman".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHollingdale1999151_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHollingdale1999151-54">[53]</a></sup> Nietzsche and SalomÃ© spent the summer together in <a href="/wiki/Tautenburg" title="Tautenburg">Tautenburg</a> in Thuringia, often with Nietzsche's sister Elisabeth as a chaperone. SalomÃ© reports that he asked her to marry him on three separate occasions and that she refused, though the reliability of her reports of events is questionable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaufmann197449_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaufmann197449-55">[54]</a></sup> Arriving in <a href="/wiki/Leipzig" title="Leipzig">Leipzig</a> (Germany) in October, SalomÃ© and RÃ©e separated from Nietzsche after a falling-out between Nietzsche and SalomÃ©, in which SalomÃ© believed that Nietzsche was desperately in love with her.
</p><p>While the three spent a number of weeks together in Leipzig in October 1882, the following month RÃ©e and SalomÃ© left Nietzsche, leaving for Stibbe (today <a href="/wiki/Zdbowo" title="Zdbowo">Zdbowo</a> in Poland)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> without any plans to meet again. Nietzsche soon fell into a period of mental anguish, although he continued to write to RÃ©e, stating "We shall see one another from time to time, won't we?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHollingdale1999152_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHollingdale1999152-57">[56]</a></sup> In later recriminations, Nietzsche would blame on separate occasions the failure in his attempts to woo SalomÃ© on SalomÃ©, RÃ©e, and on the intrigues of his sister (who had written letters to the families of SalomÃ© and RÃ©e to disrupt the plans for the commune). Nietzsche wrote of the affair in 1883, that he now felt "genuine hatred for my sister".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHollingdale1999152_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHollingdale1999152-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Amidst renewed bouts of illness, living in near-isolation after a falling out with his mother and sister regarding SalomÃ©, Nietzsche fled to Rapallo, where he wrote the first part of <i>Also Sprach Zarathustra</i> in only ten days.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche1882.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1700" data-file-width="1274" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Nietzsche1882.jpg/170px-Nietzsche1882.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Nietzsche1882.jpg/255px-Nietzsche1882.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Nietzsche1882.jpg/340px-Nietzsche1882.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche1882.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photo of Nietzsche by <a href="/wiki/Gustav-Adolf_Schultze" title="Gustav-Adolf Schultze">Gustav-Adolf Schultze</a>, 1882</div></div></div>
<p>By 1882, Nietzsche was taking huge doses of <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a>, but he was still having trouble sleeping.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECate2005389_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECate2005389-58">[57]</a></sup> In 1883, while staying in Nice, he was writing out his own prescriptions for the sedative <a href="/wiki/Chloral_hydrate" title="Chloral hydrate">chloral hydrate</a>, signing them "Dr. Nietzsche".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECate2005453_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECate2005453-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>He turned away from the influence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schopenhauer" title="Schopenhauer">Schopenhauer</a>, and after he severed his social ties with Wagner, Nietzsche had few remaining friends. Now, with the new style of <i>Zarathustra</i>, his work became even more alienating, and the market received it only to the degree required by politeness. Nietzsche recognized this and maintained his solitude, though he often complained. His books remained largely unsold. In 1885, he printed only 40 copies of the fourth part of <i>Zarathustra</i> and distributed a fraction of them among close friends, including <a href="/wiki/Helene_von_Druskowitz" title="Helene von Druskowitz">Helene von Druskowitz</a>.
</p><p>In 1883, he tried and failed to obtain a lecturing post at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Leipzig" title="University of Leipzig">University of Leipzig</a>. According to a letter he wrote to Peter Gast, this was due to his "attitude towards Christianity and the concept of God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1886, Nietzsche broke with his publisher Ernst Schmeitzner, disgusted by his antisemitic opinions. Nietzsche saw his own writings as "completely buried and in this anti-Semitic dump" of Schmeitznerâassociating the publisher with a movement that should be "utterly rejected with cold contempt by every sensible mind".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> He then printed <i><a href="/wiki/Beyond_Good_and_Evil" title="Beyond Good and Evil">Beyond Good and Evil</a></i> at his own expense. He also acquired the publication rights for his earlier works and over the next year issued second editions of <i>The Birth of Tragedy</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Human,_All_Too_Human" title="Human, All Too Human">Human, All Too Human</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dawn_of_Day" title="The Dawn of Day">Daybreak</a></i>, and of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gay_Science" title="The Gay Science">The Gay Science</a></i> with new prefaces placing the body of his work in a more coherent perspective. Thereafter, he saw his work as completed for a time and hoped that soon a readership would develop. In fact, interest in Nietzsche's thought did increase at this time, if rather slowly and imperceptibly to him. During these years Nietzsche met <a href="/wiki/Meta_von_Salis" title="Meta von Salis">Meta von Salis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Spitteler" title="Carl Spitteler">Carl Spitteler</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Keller" title="Gottfried Keller">Gottfried Keller</a>.
</p><p>In 1886, his sister Elisabeth married the <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">antisemite</a> <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_F%C3%B6rster" title="Bernhard FÃ¶rster">Bernhard FÃ¶rster</a> and travelled to Paraguay to found <a href="/wiki/Nueva_Germania" title="Nueva Germania">Nueva Germania</a>, a "Germanic" colony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> Through correspondence, Nietzsche's relationship with Elisabeth continued through cycles of conflict and reconciliation, but they met again only after his collapse. He continued to have frequent and painful attacks of illness, which made prolonged work impossible.
</p><p>In 1887, Nietzsche wrote the polemic <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Genealogy_of_Morality" title="On the Genealogy of Morality">On the Genealogy of Morality</a></i>. During the same year, he encountered the work of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky">Fyodor Dostoyevsky</a>, to whom he felt an immediate kinship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> He also exchanged letters with <a href="/wiki/Hippolyte_Taine" title="Hippolyte Taine">Hippolyte Taine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georg_Brandes" title="Georg Brandes">Georg Brandes</a>. Brandes, who had started to teach the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard" title="SÃ¸ren Kierkegaard">SÃ¸ren Kierkegaard</a> in the 1870s, wrote to Nietzsche asking him to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard_and_Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="SÃ¸ren Kierkegaard and Friedrich Nietzsche">read Kierkegaard</a>, to which Nietzsche replied that he would come to <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a> and read Kierkegaard with him. However, before fulfilling this promise, Nietzsche slipped too far into illness. At the beginning of 1888, Brandes delivered in Copenhagen one of the first lectures on Nietzsche's philosophy.
</p><p>Although Nietzsche had previously announced at the end of <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Genealogy_of_Morality" title="On the Genealogy of Morality">On the Genealogy of Morality</a></i> a new work with the title <i><a href="/wiki/The_Will_to_Power_(manuscript)" title="The Will to Power (manuscript)">The Will to Power</a>: Attempt at a <a href="/wiki/Transvaluation_of_values" title="Transvaluation of values">Revaluation of All Values</a></i>, he seems to have abandoned this idea and, instead, used some of the draft passages to compose <i><a href="/wiki/Twilight_of_the_Idols" title="Twilight of the Idols">Twilight of the Idols</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Antichrist_(book)" title="The Antichrist (book)">The Antichrist</a></i> in 1888.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>His health improved and he spent the summer in high spirits. In the autumn of 1888, his writings and letters began to reveal a higher estimation of his own status and "fate". He overestimated the increasing response to his writings, however, especially to the recent polemic, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Case_of_Wagner" title="The Case of Wagner">The Case of Wagner</a></i>. On his 44th birthday, after completing <i>Twilight of the Idols</i> and <i>The Antichrist</i>, he decided to write the autobiography <i><a href="/wiki/Ecce_Homo_(book)" title="Ecce Homo (book)">Ecce Homo</a></i>. In its prefaceâwhich suggests Nietzsche was well aware of the interpretive difficulties his work would generateâhe declares, "Hear me! For I am such and such a person. Above all, do not mistake me for someone else."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENietzsche1888dPreface,_section_1_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENietzsche1888dPreface,_section_1-66">[65]</a></sup> In December, Nietzsche began a correspondence with <a href="/wiki/August_Strindberg" title="August Strindberg">August Strindberg</a> and thought that, short of an international breakthrough, he would attempt to buy back his older writings from the publisher and have them translated into other European languages. Moreover, he planned the publication of the compilation <i><a href="/wiki/Nietzsche_contra_Wagner" title="Nietzsche contra Wagner">Nietzsche contra Wagner</a></i> and of the poems that made up his collection <i><a href="/wiki/Dionysian-Dithyrambs" title="Dionysian-Dithyrambs">Dionysian-Dithyrambs</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Insanity_and_death_.281889.E2.80.931900.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Insanity_and_death_(1889â1900)">Insanity and death (1889â1900)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Insanity and death (1889â1900)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span class="anchor" id="mental_breakdown_and_death"></span><span class="anchor" id="breakdown"></span><span class="anchor" id="death"></span>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:270px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NietzscheHouseTurin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2008" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/NietzscheHouseTurin.jpg/268px-NietzscheHouseTurin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/NietzscheHouseTurin.jpg/402px-NietzscheHouseTurin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/NietzscheHouseTurin.jpg/536px-NietzscheHouseTurin.jpg 2x" width="268"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NietzscheHouseTurin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">Turin</a> house where Nietzsche stayed (background) seen from Piazza Carlo Alberto, where he is said to have had his breakdown (at left: rear faÃ§ade of <a href="/wiki/Palazzo_Carignano" title="Palazzo Carignano">Palazzo Carignano</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>On 3 January 1889, Nietzsche suffered a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_breakdown" title="Mental breakdown">mental breakdown</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> Two policemen approached him after he caused a public disturbance in the streets of <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">Turin</a>. What happened remains unknown, but an often-repeated tale from shortly after his death states that Nietzsche witnessed the flogging of a horse at the other end of the Piazza Carlo Alberto, ran to the horse, threw his arms around its neck to protect it, then collapsed to the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaufmann197467_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaufmann197467-68">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>
In the following few days, Nietzsche sent short writingsâknown as the <i>Wahnzettel</i> or <i>Wahnbriefe</i> (literally "Delusion notes" or "letters")âto a number of friends including <a href="/wiki/Cosima_Wagner" title="Cosima Wagner">Cosima Wagner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Burckhardt" title="Jacob Burckhardt">Jacob Burckhardt</a>. Most of them were signed "<a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a>", though some were also signed "der Gekreuzigte" meaning "the crucified one". To his former colleague Burckhardt, Nietzsche wrote:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:176px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Friedrich_Nietzsche_drawn_by_Hans_Olde.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="2112" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Friedrich_Nietzsche_drawn_by_Hans_Olde.jpg/174px-Friedrich_Nietzsche_drawn_by_Hans_Olde.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Friedrich_Nietzsche_drawn_by_Hans_Olde.jpg/261px-Friedrich_Nietzsche_drawn_by_Hans_Olde.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Friedrich_Nietzsche_drawn_by_Hans_Olde.jpg/348px-Friedrich_Nietzsche_drawn_by_Hans_Olde.jpg 2x" width="174"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Friedrich_Nietzsche_drawn_by_Hans_Olde.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drawing by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Olde" title="Hans Olde">Hans Olde</a> from the photographic series <i>The Ill Nietzsche</i>, late 1899</div></div></div><blockquote><p>I have had <a href="/wiki/Caiaphas" title="Caiaphas">Caiaphas</a> put in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fetters" title="Fetters">fetters</a>. Also, last year I was crucified by the German doctors in a very drawn-out manner. <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II,_German_Emperor" title="Wilhelm II, German Emperor">Wilhelm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Bismarck</a>, and all anti-Semites abolished.</p></blockquote><p>Additionally, he commanded the German emperor to go to Rome to be shot and summoned the European powers to take military action against Germany,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> writing also that the pope should be put in jail and that he, Nietzsche, created the world and was in the process of having all anti-Semites shot dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 6 January 1889, Burckhardt showed the letter he had received from Nietzsche to Overbeck. The following day, Overbeck received a similar letter and decided that Nietzsche's friends had to bring him back to Basel. Overbeck traveled to Turin and brought Nietzsche to a psychiatric clinic in Basel. By that time Nietzsche appeared fully in the grip of a serious mental illness,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-73">[72]</a></sup> and his mother Franziska decided to transfer him to a clinic in <a href="/wiki/Jena" title="Jena">Jena</a> under the direction of <a href="/wiki/Otto_Binswanger" title="Otto Binswanger">Otto Binswanger</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> In January 1889, they proceeded with the planned release of <i><a href="/wiki/Twilight_of_the_Idols" title="Twilight of the Idols">Twilight of the Idols</a></i>, by that time already printed and bound. From November 1889 to February 1890, the art historian <a href="/wiki/Julius_Langbehn" title="Julius Langbehn">Julius Langbehn</a> attempted to cure Nietzsche, claiming that the methods of the medical doctors were ineffective in treating Nietzsche's condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> Langbehn assumed progressively greater control of Nietzsche until his secretiveness discredited him. In March 1890, Franziska removed Nietzsche from the clinic and, in May 1890, brought him to her home in Naumburg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-73">[72]</a></sup> During this process Overbeck and Gast contemplated what to do with Nietzsche's unpublished works. In February, they ordered a fifty-copy private edition of <i><a href="/wiki/Nietzsche_contra_Wagner" title="Nietzsche contra Wagner">Nietzsche contra Wagner</a></i>, but the publisher C. G. Naumann secretly printed one hundred. Overbeck and Gast decided to withhold publishing <i>The Antichrist</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ecce_Homo_(book)" title="Ecce Homo (book)">Ecce Homo</a></i> because of their more radical content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-73">[72]</a></sup> Nietzsche's reception and recognition enjoyed their first surge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1893, Nietzsche's sister Elisabeth returned from <a href="/wiki/Nueva_Germania" title="Nueva Germania">Nueva Germania</a> in Paraguay following the suicide of her husband. She studied Nietzsche's works and, piece by piece, took control of their publication. Overbeck was dismissed and Gast finally co-operated. After the death of Franziska in 1897, Nietzsche lived in <a href="/wiki/Weimar" title="Weimar">Weimar</a>, where Elisabeth cared for him and allowed visitors, including <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Steiner" title="Rudolf Steiner">Rudolf Steiner</a> (who in 1895 had written <i>Friedrich Nietzsche: a Fighter Against His Time</i>, one of the first books praising Nietzsche),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> to meet her uncommunicative brother. Elisabeth employed Steiner as a tutor to help her to understand her brother's philosophy. Steiner abandoned the attempt after only a few months, declaring that it was impossible to teach her anything about philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eh-dm-27.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="736" data-file-width="1539" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Eh-dm-27.JPG/220px-Eh-dm-27.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Eh-dm-27.JPG/330px-Eh-dm-27.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Eh-dm-27.JPG/440px-Eh-dm-27.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eh-dm-27.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>After the breakdown, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_K%C3%B6selitz" title="Heinrich KÃ¶selitz">Peter Gast</a> "corrected" Nietzsche's writings without his approval.</div></div></div>
<p>Nietzsche's insanity was originally diagnosed as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tertiary_syphilis" title="Tertiary syphilis">tertiary syphilis</a>, in accordance with a prevailing medical paradigm of the time. Although most commentators<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2021)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> regard his breakdown as unrelated to his philosophy, <a href="/wiki/Georges_Bataille" title="Georges Bataille">Georges Bataille</a> dropped dark hints ("'Man incarnate' must also go mad")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bataille_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bataille-79">[78]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Girard" title="RenÃ© Girard">RenÃ© Girard</a>'s postmortem psychoanalysis posits a worshipful rivalry with <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> Nietzsche had previously written, "All superior men who were irresistibly drawn to throw off the yoke of any kind of morality and to frame new laws had, if they were not actually mad, no alternative but to make themselves or pretend to be mad." (Daybreak, 14) The diagnosis of syphilis has since been challenged and a diagnosis of "<a href="/wiki/Bipolar_disorder" title="Bipolar disorder">manic-depressive illness</a> with periodic <a href="/wiki/Psychosis" title="Psychosis">psychosis</a> followed by <a href="/wiki/Vascular_dementia" title="Vascular dementia">vascular dementia</a>" was put forward by Cybulska prior to Schain's study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Sax" title="Leonard Sax">Leonard Sax</a> suggested the slow growth of a right-sided retro-orbital <a href="/wiki/Meningioma" title="Meningioma">meningioma</a> as an explanation of Nietzsche's dementia;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> Orth and Trimble postulated <a href="/wiki/Frontotemporal_dementia" title="Frontotemporal dementia">frontotemporal dementia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> while other researchers have proposed a hereditary stroke disorder called <a href="/wiki/CADASIL" title="CADASIL">CADASIL</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Poisoning by <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, a treatment for syphilis at the time of Nietzsche's death,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> has also been suggested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1898 and 1899, Nietzsche suffered at least two strokes. They partially paralyzed him, leaving him unable to speak or walk. He likely suffered from clinical <a href="/wiki/Hemiparesis" title="Hemiparesis">hemiparesis</a>/hemiplegia on the left side of his body by 1899. After contracting <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a> in mid-August 1900, he had another stroke during the night of 24â25 August and died at about noon on 25 August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> Elisabeth had him buried beside his father at the church in <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B6cken" title="RÃ¶cken">RÃ¶cken</a> <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCtzen" title="LÃ¼tzen">LÃ¼tzen</a>. His friend and secretary Gast gave his funeral oration, proclaiming: "Holy be your name to all future generations!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geburtshaus_Friedrich_Nietzsches_mit_Skulpturengruppe_R%C3%B6cken.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Geburtshaus_Friedrich_Nietzsches_mit_Skulpturengruppe_R%C3%B6cken.jpg/220px-Geburtshaus_Friedrich_Nietzsches_mit_Skulpturengruppe_R%C3%B6cken.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Geburtshaus_Friedrich_Nietzsches_mit_Skulpturengruppe_R%C3%B6cken.jpg/330px-Geburtshaus_Friedrich_Nietzsches_mit_Skulpturengruppe_R%C3%B6cken.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Geburtshaus_Friedrich_Nietzsches_mit_Skulpturengruppe_R%C3%B6cken.jpg/440px-Geburtshaus_Friedrich_Nietzsches_mit_Skulpturengruppe_R%C3%B6cken.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geburtshaus_Friedrich_Nietzsches_mit_Skulpturengruppe_R%C3%B6cken.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nietzsche's grave at RÃ¶cken with the sculpture <i>Das RÃ¶ckener Bacchanal</i> by Klaus Friedrich Messerschmidt (2000)</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_F%C3%B6rster-Nietzsche" title="Elisabeth FÃ¶rster-Nietzsche">Elisabeth FÃ¶rster-Nietzsche</a> compiled <i><a href="/wiki/The_Will_to_Power_(manuscript)" title="The Will to Power (manuscript)">The Will to Power</a></i> from Nietzsche's unpublished notebooks and published it posthumously in 1901. Because his sister arranged the book based on her own conflation of several of Nietzsche's early outlines and took liberties with the material, the scholarly consensus has been that it does not reflect Nietzsche's intent. (For example, Elisabeth removed aphorism 35 of <i>The Antichrist</i>, where Nietzsche rewrote a passage of the Bible.) Indeed, <a href="/wiki/Mazzino_Montinari" title="Mazzino Montinari">Mazzino Montinari</a>, the editor of Nietzsche's <i><a href="/wiki/Nachlass" title="Nachlass">Nachlass</a></i>, called it a forgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> Yet, the endeavour to rescue Nietzsche's reputation by discrediting <i>The Will to Power</i> often leads to scepticism about the value of his late notes, even of his whole <i>Nachlass</i>. However, his <i>Nachlass</i> and <i>The Will to Power</i> are distinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Citizenship.2C_nationality_and_ethnicity"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Citizenship,_nationality_and_ethnicity">Citizenship, nationality and ethnicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Citizenship, nationality and ethnicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>General commentators and Nietzsche scholars, whether emphasizing his cultural background or his language, overwhelmingly label Nietzsche as a "German philosopher".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEP-92">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagnus1999_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagnus1999-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup> Others do not assign him a national category.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> While Germany had not yet been unified into a nation-state, Nietzsche was born a citizen of <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>, which was mostly part of the <a href="/wiki/German_Confederation" title="German Confederation">German Confederation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mencken2008_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mencken2008-98">[97]</a></sup> His birthplace, <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B6cken" title="RÃ¶cken">RÃ¶cken</a>, is in the modern German state of <a href="/wiki/Saxony-Anhalt" title="Saxony-Anhalt">Saxony-Anhalt</a>. When he accepted his post at Basel, Nietzsche applied for annulment of his Prussian citizenship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> The official revocation of his citizenship came in a document dated 17 April 1869,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> and for the rest of his life he remained officially <a href="/wiki/Statelessness" title="Statelessness">stateless</a>.
</p><p>At least toward the end of his life, Nietzsche believed his ancestors were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polish_people" title="Polish people">Polish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mencken1913_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mencken1913-101">[100]</a></sup> He wore a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signet_ring" title="Signet ring">signet ring</a> bearing the <a href="/wiki/Radwan_coat_of_arms" title="Radwan coat of arms">Radwan coat of arms</a>, traceable back to <a href="/wiki/Szlachta" title="Szlachta">Polish nobility</a> of medieval times<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nietzsche-radwan-ring_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-nietzsche-radwan-ring-102">[101]</a></sup> and the surname "Nicki" of the Polish noble (<a href="/wiki/Szlachta" title="Szlachta">szlachta</a>) family bearing that coat of arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> Gotard Nietzsche, a member of the Nicki family, left Poland for <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>. His descendants later settled in the <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Saxony" title="Electorate of Saxony">Electorate of Saxony</a> circa the year 1700.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> Nietzsche wrote in 1888, "My ancestors were Polish noblemen (Nietzky); the type seems to have been well preserved despite three generations of German mothers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHollingdale19996_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHollingdale19996-106">[105]</a></sup> At one point, Nietzsche becomes even more adamant about his Polish identity. "I am a pure-blooded Polish nobleman, without a single drop of bad blood, certainly not German blood."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> On yet another occasion, Nietzsche stated, "Germany is a great nation only because its people have so much Polish blood in their veins.... I am proud of my Polish descent."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> Nietzsche believed his name might have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanized" title="Germanized">Germanized</a>, in one letter claiming, "I was taught to ascribe the origin of my blood and name to Polish noblemen who were called NiÃ«tzky and left their home and nobleness about a hundred years ago, finally yielding to unbearable suppression: they were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestants" title="Protestants">Protestants</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most scholars dispute Nietzsche's account of his family's origins.
