Hans von MÃ¼ller debunked the genealogy put forward by Nietzsche's sister in favor of Polish noble heritage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Max_Oehler" title="Max Oehler">Max Oehler</a>, Nietzsche's cousin and curator of the <a href="/wiki/Nietzsche_Archive" title="Nietzsche Archive">Nietzsche Archive</a> at <a href="/wiki/Weimar" title="Weimar">Weimar</a>, argued that all of Nietzsche's ancestors bore German names, including the wives' families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHollingdale19996_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHollingdale19996-106">[105]</a></sup> Oehler claims that Nietzsche came from a long line of German <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> clergymen on both sides of his family, and modern scholars regard the claim of Nietzsche's Polish ancestry as "pure invention".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mencken_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-mencken-111">[110]</a></sup> Colli and Montinari, the editors of Nietzsche's assembled letters, gloss Nietzsche's claims as a "mistaken belief" and "without foundation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup> The name <i>Nietzsche</i> itself is not a Polish name, but an exceptionally common one throughout central Germany, in this and cognate forms (such as <i>Nitsche</i> and <i>Nitzke</i>). The name derives from the forename <i>Nikolaus,</i> abbreviated to <i>Nick</i>; assimilated with the Slavic <i>Nitz</i>; it first became <i>Nitsche</i> and then <i>Nietzsche</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHollingdale19996_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHollingdale19996-106">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is not known why Nietzsche wanted to be thought of as Polish nobility. According to biographer <a href="/wiki/R._J._Hollingdale" title="R. J. Hollingdale">R. J. Hollingdale</a>, Nietzsche's propagation of the Polish ancestry myth may have been part of his "campaign against Germany".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHollingdale19996_106-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHollingdale19996-106">[105]</a></sup> Nicholas D. More states that Nietzsche's claims of having an illustrious lineage were a parody on autobiographical conventions, and suspects <i>Ecce Homo</i>, with its self-laudatory titles, such as "<i>Why I Am So Wise</i>", as being a work of satire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-114">[113]</a></sup> He concludes that Nietzsche's supposed Polish genealogy was a jokeânot a delusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-114">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationships_and_sexuality">Relationships and sexuality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Relationships and sexuality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nietzsche never married. He proposed to <a href="/wiki/Lou_Andreas-Salom%C3%A9" title="Lou Andreas-SalomÃ©">Lou SalomÃ©</a> three times and each time was rejected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> One theory blames SalomÃ©'s view on sexuality as one of the reasons for her alienation from Nietzsche. As articulated in her 1898 novella <i>Fenitschka</i>, SalomÃ© viewed the idea of sexual intercourse as prohibitive and marriage as a violation, with some suggesting that they indicated <a href="/wiki/Sexual_repression" title="Sexual repression">sexual repression</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neurosis" title="Neurosis">neurosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup> Reflecting on <a href="/wiki/Unrequited_love" title="Unrequited love">unrequited love</a>, Nietzsche considered that "indispensable ... to the lover is his unrequited love, which he would at no price relinquish for a state of indifference".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[ii]</a></sup>
</p><p>Deussen cited the episode of <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>'s brothel in February 1865 as instrumental to understanding the philosopher's way of thinking, mostly about women. Nietzsche was surreptitiously accompanied to a "call house" from which he clumsily escaped upon seeing "a half dozen apparitions dressed in sequins and veils." According to Deussen, Nietzsche "never decided to remain unmarried all his life. For him, women had to sacrifice themselves to the care and benefit of men."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-42">[41]</a></sup> Nietzsche scholar <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Joachim_K%C3%B6hler&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Joachim KÃ¶hler (page does not exist)">Joachim KÃ¶hler</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">Â [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joachim_K%C3%B6hler_(Philosoph)" title="de:Joachim KÃ¶hler (Philosoph)">de</a>]</span> has attempted to explain Nietzsche's life history and philosophy by claiming that he was homosexual. KÃ¶hler argues that Nietzsche's supposed syphilis, which is "...<span class="nowrap">Â </span>usually considered to be the product of his encounter with a prostitute in a brothel in <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> or <a href="/wiki/Leipzig" title="Leipzig">Leipzig</a>, is equally likely. Some maintain that Nietzsche contracted it in a male brothel in <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[116]</a></sup> The acquisition of the infection from a homosexual brothel was the theory believed by <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>, who cited Otto Binswanger as his source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-119">[117]</a></sup> KÃ¶hler also suggests Nietzsche may have had a romantic relationship, as well as a friendship, with <a href="/wiki/Paul_R%C3%A9e" title="Paul RÃ©e">Paul RÃ©e</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Megill_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Megill-120">[118]</a></sup> There is the claim that Nietzsche's homosexuality was widely known in the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Psychoanalytic_Society" title="Vienna Psychoanalytic Society">Vienna Psychoanalytic Society</a>, with Nietzsche's friend <a href="/wiki/Paul_Deussen" title="Paul Deussen">Paul Deussen</a> claiming that "he was a man who had never touched a woman."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>KÃ¶hler's views have not found wide acceptance among Nietzsche scholars and commentators. Allan Megill argues that, while KÃ¶hler's claim that Nietzsche was conflicted about his homosexual desire cannot simply be dismissed, "the evidence is very weak," and KÃ¶hler may be projecting twentieth-century understandings of sexuality on nineteenth-century notions of friendship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Megill_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-Megill-120">[118]</a></sup> It is also rumored that Nietzsche frequented <a href="/wiki/Heterosexuality" title="Heterosexuality">heterosexual</a> brothels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-119">[117]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Rodgers" title="Nigel Rodgers">Nigel Rodgers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mel_Thompson_(writer)" title="Mel Thompson (writer)">Mel Thompson</a> have argued that continuous sickness and headaches hindered Nietzsche from engaging much with women. Yet they offer other examples in which Nietzsche expressed his affections to women, including Wagner's wife <a href="/wiki/Cosima_Wagner" title="Cosima Wagner">Cosima Wagner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other scholars have argued that KÃ¶hler's sexuality-based interpretation is not helpful in understanding Nietzsche's philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[123]</a></sup> However, there are also those who stress that, if Nietzsche preferred menâwith this preference constituting his <a href="/wiki/Psychosexual_development" title="Psychosexual development">psycho-sexual</a> make-upâbut could not admit his desires to himself, it meant he acted in conflict with his philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche187c.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2940" data-file-width="1956" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Nietzsche187c.jpg/170px-Nietzsche187c.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Nietzsche187c.jpg/255px-Nietzsche187c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Nietzsche187c.jpg/340px-Nietzsche187c.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche187c.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nietzsche, 1869</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Philosophy of Friedrich Nietzsche">Philosophy of Friedrich Nietzsche</a></div>
<p>Because of Nietzsche's evocative style and provocative ideas, his philosophy generates passionate reactions. His works remain controversial, due to varying interpretations and misinterpretations. In Western philosophy, Nietzsche's writings have been described as a case of free revolutionary thought, that is, revolutionary in its structure and problems, although not tied to any revolutionary project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennett2001_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bennett2001-127">[125]</a></sup> His writings have also been described as a revolutionary project in which his philosophy serves as the foundation of a European cultural rebirth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Apollonian_and_Dionysian">Apollonian and Dionysian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Apollonian and Dionysian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Apollonian_and_Dionysian" title="Apollonian and Dionysian">Apollonian and Dionysian</a></div>
<p>The <i>Apollonian and Dionysian</i> is a two-fold philosophical concept, based on features of ancient Greek mythology: <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a>. This relationship takes the form of a <a href="/wiki/Dialectic" title="Dialectic">dialectic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup> Even though the concept is related to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Birth_of_Tragedy" title="The Birth of Tragedy">The Birth of Tragedy</a>,</i> the poet <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_H%C3%B6lderlin" title="Friedrich HÃ¶lderlin">HÃ¶lderlin</a> had already spoken of it, and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Joachim_Winckelmann" title="Johann Joachim Winckelmann">Winckelmann</a> had talked of <a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Bacchus</a>.
</p><p>Nietzsche found in classical Athenian tragedy an art form that <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(philosophy)" title="Transcendence (philosophy)">transcended</a> the pessimism found in the so-called <a href="/wiki/Silenus#The_wisdom_of_Silenus" title="Silenus">wisdom of Silenus</a>. The Greek spectators, by looking into the abyss of human suffering depicted by characters on stage, passionately and joyously affirmed life, finding it worth living. The main theme in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Birth_of_Tragedy" title="The Birth of Tragedy">The Birth of Tragedy</a></i> is that the fusion of Dionysian and Apollonian <i>Kunsttriebe</i> ("artistic impulses") forms dramatic arts or tragedies. He argued that this fusion has not been achieved since the ancient Greek <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tragedians" title="Tragedians">tragedians</a>. Apollo represents harmony, progress, clarity, logic and the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_individuation" title="Principle of individuation">principle of individuation</a>, whereas Dionysus represents disorder, intoxication, emotion, ecstasy and unity (hence the omission of the principle of individuation). Nietzsche used these two forces because, for him, the world of mind and order on one side, and passion and chaos on the other, formed principles that were fundamental to the <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Greece" title="Culture of Greece">Greek culture</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup> the Apollonian a dreaming state, full of illusions; and Dionysian a state of intoxication, representing the liberations of instinct and dissolution of boundaries. In this mold, a man appears as the <a href="/wiki/Satyr" title="Satyr">satyr</a>. He is the horror of the annihilation of the principle of <a href="/wiki/Individuation" title="Individuation">individuality</a> and at the same time someone who delights in its destruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup> Both of these principles are meant to represent <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognitive</a> states that appear through art as the power of nature in man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>Apollonian and Dionysian juxtapositions appear in the interplay of tragedy: the tragic hero of the drama, the main protagonist, struggles to make (Apollonian) order of his unjust and chaotic (Dionysian) fate, though he dies unfulfilled. Elaborating on the conception of <a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a> as an intellectual who cannot make up his mind, and is a living <a href="/wiki/Antithesis" title="Antithesis">antithesis</a> to the man of action, Nietzsche argues that a Dionysian figure possesses the knowledge that his actions cannot change the eternal balance of things, and it disgusts him enough not to act at all. Hamlet falls under this categoryâhe glimpsed the supernatural reality through the Ghost, he has gained true knowledge and knows that no action of his has the power to change this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup> For the audience of such drama, this tragedy allows them to sense what Nietzsche called the <i>Primordial Unity</i>, which revives Dionysian nature. He describes primordial unity as the increase of strength, the experience of fullness and plenitude bestowed by <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/frenzy#Noun" title="wikt:frenzy">frenzy</a>. Frenzy acts as intoxication and is crucial for the <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiological</a> condition that enables the creation of any art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-137">[135]</a></sup> Stimulated by this state, a person's artistic will is enhanced:
</p>
<blockquote><p>In this state one enriches everything out of one's own fullness: whatever one sees, whatever wills is seen swelled, taut, strong, overloaded with strength. A man in this state transforms things until they mirror his powerâuntil they are reflections of his perfection. This having to transform into perfection isâart.</p></blockquote>
<p>Nietzsche is adamant that the works of <a href="/wiki/Aeschylus" title="Aeschylus">Aeschylus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sophocles" title="Sophocles">Sophocles</a> represent the apex of artistic creation, the true realization of tragedy; it is with <a href="/wiki/Euripides" title="Euripides">Euripides</a>, that tragedy begins its <i>Untergang</i> (literally 'going under' or 'downward-way;' meaning decline, deterioration, downfall, death, etc.). Nietzsche objects to Euripides' use of <a href="/wiki/Socratic_method" title="Socratic method">Socratic rationalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a> in his tragedies, claiming that the infusion of ethics and <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a> robs tragedy of its foundation, namely the fragile balance of the Dionysian and Apollonian. <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> emphasized reason to such a degree that he diffused the value of myth and suffering to human knowledge. <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> continued along this path in his dialogues, and the modern world eventually inherited reason at the expense of artistic impulses found in the Apollonian and Dionysian dichotomy. He notes that without the Apollonian, the Dionysian lacks the form and structure to make a coherent piece of art, and without the Dionysian, the Apollonian lacks the necessary vitality and passion. Only the fertile interplay of these two forces brought together as an art represented the best of Greek tragedy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>An example of the impact of this idea can be seen in the book <i>Patterns of Culture,</i> where <a href="/wiki/Anthropologist" title="Anthropologist">anthropologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Ruth_Benedict" title="Ruth Benedict">Ruth Benedict</a> acknowledges Nietzschean opposites of "Apollonian" and "Dionysian" as the stimulus for her thoughts about <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_American_culture" title="Native American culture">Native American cultures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Jung" title="Carl Jung">Carl Jung</a> has written extensively on the dichotomy in <i><a href="/wiki/Psychological_Types" title="Psychological Types">Psychological Types</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michel_Foucault" title="Michel Foucault">Michel Foucault</a> commented that his own book <i><a href="/wiki/Madness_and_Civilization" title="Madness and Civilization">Madness and Civilization</a></i> should be read "under the sun of the great Nietzschean inquiry". Here Foucault referenced Nietzsche's description of the birth and death of tragedy and his explanation that the subsequent tragedy of the Western world was the refusal of the tragic and, with that, refusal of the sacred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup> Painter <a href="/wiki/Mark_Rothko" title="Mark Rothko">Mark Rothko</a> was influenced by Nietzsche's view of tragedy presented in <i>The Birth of Tragedy.</i>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Perspectivism">Perspectivism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Perspectivism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Perspectivism" title="Perspectivism">Perspectivism</a></div>
<p>Nietzsche claimed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_of_God" title="Death of God">death of God</a> would eventually lead to the loss of any universal perspective on things and any coherent sense of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Objective_truth" title="Objective truth">objective truth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELampert198617â18_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELampert198617â18-143">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeidegger_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeidegger-144">[142]</a></sup> Nietzsche rejected the idea of objective reality, arguing that knowledge is <a href="/wiki/Contingency_(philosophy)" title="Contingency (philosophy)">contingent</a> and conditional, relative to various fluid perspectives or interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup> This leads to constant reassessment of rules (i.e., those of philosophy, the scientific method, etc.) according to the circumstances of individual perspectives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup> This view has acquired the name <i><a href="/wiki/Perspectivism" title="Perspectivism">perspectivism</a>.</i>
</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Thus_Spoke_Zarathustra" title="Thus Spoke Zarathustra">Thus Spoke Zarathustra</a>,</i> Nietzsche proclaimed that a table of values hangs above every great person. He pointed out that what is common among different peoples is the act of esteeming, of creating values, even if the values are different from one person to the next. Nietzsche asserted that what made people great was not the content of their beliefs, but the act of valuing. Thus the values a community strives to articulate are not as important as the collective will to see those values come to pass. The willingness is more essential than the merit of the goal itself, according to Nietzsche. "A thousand goals have there been so far", says Zarathustra, "for there are a thousand peoples. Only the yoke for the thousand necks is still lacking: the one goal is lacking. Humanity still has no goal." Hence, the title of the aphorism, "On The Thousand And One Goal". The idea that one value-system is no more worthy than the next, although it may not be directly ascribed to Nietzsche, has become a common premise in modern social science. <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Max Weber</a> and <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heidegger" title="Martin Heidegger">Martin Heidegger</a> absorbed it and made it their own. It shaped their philosophical and cultural endeavors, as well as their political understanding. Weber, for example, relied on Nietzsche's perspectivism by maintaining that objectivity is still possibleâbut only after a particular perspective, value, or end has been established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among his critique of traditional philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Kant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="RenÃ© Descartes">Descartes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Beyond_Good_and_Evil" title="Beyond Good and Evil">Beyond Good and Evil</a>,</i> Nietzsche attacked the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thing_in_itself" title="Thing in itself">thing in itself</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cogito_ergo_sum" title="Cogito ergo sum">cogito ergo sum</a></i> ("I think, therefore I am") as <a href="/wiki/Falsifiability" title="Falsifiability">unfalsifiable</a> beliefs based on naive acceptance of previous notions and <a href="/wiki/Fallacy" title="Fallacy">fallacies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[147]</a></sup> Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Alasdair_MacIntyre" title="Alasdair MacIntyre">Alasdair MacIntyre</a> put Nietzsche in a high place in the history of philosophy. While criticizing nihilism and Nietzsche together as a sign of general decay,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[148]</a></sup> he still commended him for recognizing psychological motives behind Kant and <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">Hume</a>'s moral philosophy:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>For it was Nietzsche's historic achievement to understand more clearly than any other philosopher ... not only that what purported to be appeals of <a href="/wiki/Objectivity_(philosophy)" title="Objectivity (philosophy)">objectivity</a> were in fact expressions of subjective will, but also the nature of the problems that this posed for philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slave_revolt_in_morals">Slave revolt in morals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Slave revolt in morals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Master%E2%80%93slave_morality" title="Masterâslave morality">Masterâslave morality</a></div>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Beyond_Good_and_Evil" title="Beyond Good and Evil">Beyond Good and Evil</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Genealogy_of_Morality" title="On the Genealogy of Morality">On the Genealogy of Morality</a>,</i> Nietzsche's <a href="/wiki/Genealogy_(philosophy)" title="Genealogy (philosophy)">genealogical</a> account of the development of modern moral systems occupies a central place. For Nietzsche, a fundamental shift took place during the human history from thinking in terms of "good and bad" toward "good and evil".
</p><p>The initial form of morality was set by a warrior <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy_(class)" title="Aristocracy (class)">aristocracy</a> and other ruling castes of ancient civilizations. Aristocratic values of good and bad coincided with and reflected their relationship to lower <a href="/wiki/Caste" title="Caste">castes</a> such as slaves. Nietzsche presented this "master morality" as the original system of moralityâperhaps best associated with <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homeric</a> Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LacewingSlave_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-LacewingSlave-153">[151]</a></sup> To be "good" was to be happy and to have the things related to happiness: wealth, strength, health, power, etc. To be "bad" was to be like the slaves over whom the aristocracy ruled: poor, weak, sick, patheticâobjects of pity or disgust rather than hatred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>"Slave morality" developed as a reaction to master morality. Value emerges from the contrast between good and evil: good being associated with other-worldliness, charity, piety, restraint, meekness, and submission; while evil is worldly, cruel, selfish, wealthy, and aggressive. Nietzsche saw slave morality as pessimistic and fearful, its values emerging to improve the self-perception of slaves. He associated slave morality with the Jewish and Christian traditions, as it is born out of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ressentiment" title="Ressentiment">ressentiment</a></i> of slaves. Nietzsche argued that the idea of equality allowed slaves to overcome their own conditions without despising themselves. By denying the inherent inequality of peopleâin success, strength, beauty, and intelligenceâslaves acquired a method of escape, namely by generating new values on the basis of rejecting master morality, which frustrated them. It was used to overcome the slave's sense of inferiority before their (better-off) masters. It does so by making out slave weakness, for example, to be a matter of choice, by relabeling it as "meekness". The "good man" of master morality is precisely the "evil man" of slave morality, while the "bad man" is recast as the "good man".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LacewingSlave_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-LacewingSlave-153">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nietzsche saw slave morality as a source of the nihilism that has overtaken Europe. Modern Europe and Christianity exist in a hypocritical state due to a tension between master and slave morality, both contradictory values determining, to varying degrees, the values of most Europeans (who are "<a href="/wiki/Motley" title="Motley">motley</a>"). Nietzsche called for exceptional people not to be ashamed in the face of a supposed morality-for-all, which he deems to be harmful to the flourishing of exceptional people. He cautioned, however, that morality, per se, is not bad; it is good for the masses and should be left to them. Exceptional people, on the other hand, should follow their own "inner law".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LacewingSlave_153-2"><a href="#cite_note-LacewingSlave-153">[151]</a></sup> A favorite motto of Nietzsche, taken from <a href="/wiki/Pindar" title="Pindar">Pindar</a>, reads: "Become what you are."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KYLook_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-KYLook-155">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>A long-standing assumption about Nietzsche is that he preferred master over slave morality. However, eminent Nietzsche scholar <a href="/wiki/Walter_Kaufmann_(philosopher)" title="Walter Kaufmann (philosopher)">Walter Kaufmann</a> rejected this interpretation, writing that Nietzsche's analyses of these two types of morality were used only in a <a href="/wiki/Descriptive_ethics" title="Descriptive ethics">descriptive</a> and historic sense; they were not meant for any kind of acceptance or glorification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup> On the other hand, Nietzsche called master morality "a higher order of values, the noble ones, those that say Yes to life, those that guarantee the future".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[155]</a></sup> Just as "there is an order of rank between man and man", there is also an order of rank "between morality and morality".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[156]</a></sup> Nietzsche waged a philosophic war against the slave morality of Christianity in his "revaluation of all values" to bring about the victory of a new master morality that he called the "philosophy of the future" (<i>Beyond Good and Evil</i> is subtitled <i>Prelude to a Philosophy of the Future</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Dawn_(book)" title="The Dawn (book)">Daybreak</a>,</i> Nietzsche began his "Campaign against Morality".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaufmann1974187_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaufmann1974187-160">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENietzsche1888dM_I_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENietzsche1888dM_I-161">[159]</a></sup> He called himself an "immoralist" and harshly criticized the prominent moral philosophies of his day: Christianity, <a href="/wiki/Kantianism" title="Kantianism">Kantianism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarianism</a>. Nietzsche's concept "<a href="/wiki/God_is_dead" title="God is dead">God is dead</a>" applies to the doctrines of <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a>, though not to all other faiths: he claimed that <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> is a successful religion that he complimented for fostering critical thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESedgwick200926_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESedgwick200926-162">[160]</a></sup> Still, Nietzsche saw his philosophy as a counter-movement to nihilism through appreciation of art:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Art as the single superior counterforce against all will to negation of life, art as the anti-Christian, anti-Buddhist, anti-Nihilist par excellence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-137">[135]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Nietzsche claimed that the Christian faith as practiced was not a proper representation of Jesus' teachings, as it forced people merely to believe in the way of Jesus but not to act as Jesus did; in particular, his example of refusing to judge people, something that Christians constantly did.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESedgwick200926_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESedgwick200926-162">[160]</a></sup> He condemned institutionalized Christianity for emphasizing a morality of <a href="/wiki/Pity" title="Pity">pity</a> (<i>Mitleid</i>), which assumes an inherent illness in society:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESedgwick200927_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESedgwick200927-163">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Christianity is called the religion of <i>pity.</i> Pity stands opposed to the tonic emotions which heighten our vitality: it has a depressing effect. We are deprived of strength when we feel pity. That loss of strength in which suffering as such inflicts on life is still further increased and multiplied by pity. Pity makes suffering contagious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[162]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Ecce_Homo_(book)" title="Ecce Homo (book)">Ecce Homo</a></i> Nietzsche called the establishment of moral systems based on a dichotomy of <a href="/wiki/Good_and_evil" title="Good and evil">good and evil</a> a "calamitous error",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENietzsche1888dWhy_I_Am_a_Destiny,_Â§&amp;nbsp;3_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENietzsche1888dWhy_I_Am_a_Destiny,_Â§&amp;nbsp;3-165">[163]</a></sup> and wished to initiate a <a href="/wiki/Transvaluation_of_values" title="Transvaluation of values">re-evaluation</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Value_(ethics)" title="Value (ethics)">values</a> of the Christian world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENietzsche1888c4,_8,_18,_29,_37,_40,_51,_57,_59_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENietzsche1888c4,_8,_18,_29,_37,_40,_51,_57,_59-166">[164]</a></sup> He indicated his desire to bring about a new, more naturalistic source of value in the vital impulses of life itself.
</p><p>While Nietzsche attacked the principles of Judaism, he was not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antisemitic" title="Antisemitic">antisemitic</a>: in his work <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Genealogy_of_Morality" title="On the Genealogy of Morality">On the Genealogy of Morality</a>,</i> he explicitly condemned antisemitism and pointed out that his attack on Judaism was not an attack on contemporary Jewish people but specifically an attack upon the ancient Jewish priesthood who he claimed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antisemitic_Christians" title="Antisemitic Christians">antisemitic Christians</a> paradoxically based their views upon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESedgwick200969_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESedgwick200969-167">[165]</a></sup> An Israeli historian who performed a statistical analysis of everything Nietzsche wrote about Jews claims that cross-references and context make clear that 85% of the negative comments are attacks on Christian doctrine or, sarcastically, on Richard Wagner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GolanNietzsche_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-GolanNietzsche-168">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nietzsche felt that modern antisemitism was "despicable" and contrary to European ideals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESedgwick200968_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESedgwick200968-169">[167]</a></sup> Its cause, in his opinion, was the growth in European nationalism and the endemic "jealousy and hatred" of Jewish success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESedgwick200968_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESedgwick200968-169">[167]</a></sup> He wrote that Jews should be thanked for helping uphold a respect for the philosophies of ancient Greece,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESedgwick200968_169-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESedgwick200968-169">[167]</a></sup> and for giving rise to "the noblest human being (Christ), the purest philosopher (<a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a>), the mightiest book, and the most effective moral code in the world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nebraska_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nebraska-170">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_of_God_and_nihilism">Death of God and nihilism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Death of God and nihilism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/God_is_dead" title="God is dead">God is dead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nihilism" title="Nihilism">Nihilism</a></div>
<p>The statement "<a href="/wiki/God_is_dead" title="God is dead">God is dead</a>," occurring in several of Nietzsche's works (notably in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gay_Science" title="The Gay Science">The Gay Science</a></i>), has become one of his best-known remarks. On the basis of it, many commentators<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[169]</a></sup> regard Nietzsche as an <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheist</a>; others (such as Kaufmann) suggest that this statement reflects a more subtle understanding of divinity. Scientific developments and the increasing <a href="/wiki/Secularization" title="Secularization">secularization</a> of Europe had effectively 'killed' the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abrahamic" title="Abrahamic">Abrahamic</a> God, who had served as the basis for meaning and value in the West for more than a thousand years. The death of God may lead beyond bare perspectivism to outright <a href="/wiki/Nihilism" title="Nihilism">nihilism</a>, the belief that nothing has any inherent importance and that life lacks purpose. Nietzsche believed that Christian moral doctrine provides people with <a href="/wiki/Intrinsic_value_(ethics)" title="Intrinsic value (ethics)">intrinsic value</a>, belief in God (which <a href="/wiki/Theodicy" title="Theodicy">justifies</a> the evil in the world), and a basis for <a href="/wiki/Objectivity_(philosophy)" title="Objectivity (philosophy)">objective knowledge</a>. In constructing a world where objective knowledge is possible, Christianity is an antidote to a primal form of nihilismâthe despair of meaninglessness. As <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heidegger" title="Martin Heidegger">Heidegger</a> put the problem, "If God as the supra sensory ground and goal of all reality is dead if the supra sensory world of the ideas has suffered the loss of its obligatory and above it its vitalizing and upbuilding power, then nothing more remains to which man can cling and by which he can orient himself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeidegger61_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeidegger61-172">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>One such reaction to the loss of meaning is what Nietzsche called <i>passive nihilism,</i> which he recognized in the <a href="/wiki/Pessimism" title="Pessimism">pessimistic</a> philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Schopenhauer</a>. Schopenhauer's doctrineâwhich Nietzsche also referred to as <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_the_West#Philosophical_interest" title="Buddhism in the West">Western Buddhism</a>âadvocates separating oneself from will and desires to reduce suffering. Nietzsche characterized this <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">ascetic</a> attitude as a "will to nothingness". Life turns away from itself as there is nothing of value to be found in the world. This moving away of all value in the world is characteristic of the nihilist, although, in this, the nihilist appears to be inconsistent; this "will to nothingness" is still a (disavowed) form of willing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>A nihilist is a man who judges that the real world ought <i>not</i> to be and that the world as it ought to do not exist. According to this view, our existence (action, <a href="/wiki/Suffering" title="Suffering">suffering</a>, willing, feeling) has no meaning: this 'in vain' is the nihilists' pathosâan inconsistency on the part of the nihilists.</p><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite>Friedrich Nietzsche, KSA 12:9 [60], taken from <i>The Will to Power</i>, section 585, translated by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Kaufmann_(philosopher)" title="Walter Kaufmann (philosopher)">Walter Kaufmann</a></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Nietzsche approached the problem of nihilism as a deeply personal one, stating that this problem of the modern world had "become conscious" in him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[172]</a></sup> Furthermore, he emphasized the danger of nihilism and the possibilities it offers, as seen in his statement that "I praise, I do not reproach, [nihilism's] arrival. I believe it is one of the greatest crises, a moment of the deepest self-reflection of humanity. Whether man recovers from it, whether he becomes a master of this crisis, is a question of his strength!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[173]</a></sup> According to Nietzsche, it is only when nihilism is <i>overcome</i> that a culture can have a true foundation on which to thrive. He wished to hasten its coming only so that he could also hasten its ultimate departure. Heidegger interpreted the death of God with what he explained as the death of <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>. He concluded that metaphysics has reached its potential and that the ultimate fate and downfall of metaphysics was proclaimed with the statement "God is dead."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Keiji_Nishitani" title="Keiji Nishitani">Nishitani</a> and <a href="/wiki/Graham_Parkes" title="Graham Parkes">Parkes</a> have aligned Nietzsche's religious thought with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> thinkers, particularly those of the <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana</a> tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[175]</a></sup> Occasionally, Nietzsche has also been considered in relation to Catholic mystics such as <a href="/wiki/Meister_Eckhart" title="Meister Eckhart">Meister Eckhart</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[176]</a></sup> Milne has argued against such interpretations on the grounds that such thinkers from Western and Eastern religious traditions strongly emphasise the divestment of will and the loss of ego, while Nietzsche offers a robust defence of egoism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[177]</a></sup> Milne argues that Nietzscheâs religious thought is better understood in relation to his self-professed ancestors: â<a href="/wiki/Heraclitus" title="Heraclitus">Heraclitus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Empedocles" title="Empedocles">Empedocles</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinoza" title="Spinoza">Spinoza</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goethe" title="Goethe">Goethe</a>â.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[178]</a></sup> Milne plays particularly close attention to Nietzscheâs relationship to Goethe, who has typically been neglected in research by academic philosophers. Milne shows that Goetheâs views on the one and the many allow a reciprocal determinism between part and whole, meaning that a claimed identity between part and whole does not give the part value solely in terms of belonging to the whole. In essence, this allows for a unitive sense of the individualâs relationship to the universe, while also fostering a sense of âself-esteemâ which Nietzsche found lacking in mystics such as Eckhart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[179]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Will_to_power">Will to power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Will to power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Will_to_power" title="Will to power">Will to power</a></div>
<p>A basic element in Nietzsche's philosophical outlook is the "<a href="/wiki/Will_to_power" title="Will to power">will to power</a>" (<i>der Wille zur Macht</i>), which he maintained provides a basis for understanding human behaviorâmore so than competing explanations, such as the ones based on pressure for adaptation or survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENietzsche188613_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENietzsche188613-182">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENietzsche1887II:12_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENietzsche1887II:12-183">[181]</a></sup> As such, according to Nietzsche, the drive for conservation appears as the major motivator of human or animal behavior only in exceptions, as the general condition of life is not one of a 'struggle for existence.'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENietzsche1888bSkirmishes_of_an_untimely_man,_Â§&amp;nbsp;14_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENietzsche1888bSkirmishes_of_an_untimely_man,_Â§&amp;nbsp;14-184">[182]</a></sup> More often than not, self-conservation is a consequence of a creature's will to exert its strength on the outside world.
</p><p>In presenting his theory of human behavior, Nietzsche also addressed and attacked concepts from philosophies then popularly embraced, such as Schopenhauer's notion of an aimless will or that of <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarianism</a>. Utilitarians claim that what moves people is the desire to be happy and accumulate pleasure in their lives. But such a conception of happiness Nietzsche rejected as something limited to, and characteristic of, the bourgeois lifestyle of the English society,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[183]</a></sup> and instead put forth the idea that happiness is not an aim <i>per se</i>. It is a consequence of overcoming hurdles to one's actions and the fulfillment of the will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENietzsche1888cÂ§&amp;nbsp;2_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENietzsche1888cÂ§&amp;nbsp;2-186">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>Related to his theory of the will to power is his speculation, which he did not deem final,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENietzsche1886I,_Â§&amp;nbsp;36_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENietzsche1886I,_Â§&amp;nbsp;36-187">[185]</a></sup> regarding the reality of the physical world, including inorganic matterâthat, like man's affections and impulses, the material world is also set by the dynamics of a form of the will to power. At the core of his theory is a rejection of <a href="/wiki/Atomism" title="Atomism">atomism</a>âthe idea that matter is composed of stable, indivisible units (atoms). Instead, he seemed to have accepted the conclusions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ru%C4%91er_Bo%C5%A1kovi%C4%87" title="RuÄer BoÅ¡koviÄ">RuÄer BoÅ¡koviÄ</a>, who explained the qualities of matter as a result of an interplay of forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[iii]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENietzsche1886I,_Â§&amp;nbsp;12_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENietzsche1886I,_Â§&amp;nbsp;12-189">[186]</a></sup> One study of Nietzsche defines his fully developed concept of the will to power as "the element from which derive both the quantitative difference of related forces and the quality that devolves into each force in this relation" revealing the will to power as "the principle of the synthesis of forces".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeleuze200646_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeleuze200646-190">[187]</a></sup> Of such forces Nietzsche said they could perhaps be viewed as a primitive form of the will. Likewise, he rejected the view that the movement of bodies is ruled by inexorable laws of nature, positing instead that movement was governed by the power relations between bodies and forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENietzsche1886I,_Â§&amp;nbsp;22_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENietzsche1886I,_Â§&amp;nbsp;22-191">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other scholars disagree that Nietzsche considered the material world to be a form of the will to power: Nietzsche thoroughly criticized metaphysics, and by including the will to power in the material world, he would simply be setting up a new metaphysics. Other than Aphorism 36 in <i>Beyond Good and Evil,</i> where he raised a question regarding will to power as being in the material world, they argue, it was only in his notes (unpublished by himself), where he wrote about a metaphysical will to power. And they also claim that Nietzsche directed his landlord to burn those notes in 1888 when he left Sils Maria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[189]</a></sup> According to these scholars, the "burning" story supports their thesis that Nietzsche rejected his project on the will to power at the end of his lucid life. However, a recent study (Huang 2019) shows that although it is true that in 1888 Nietzsche wanted some of his notes burned, this indicates little about his project on the will to power, not only because only 11 "aphorisms" saved from the flames were ultimately incorporated into <i>The Will to Power</i> (this book contains 1067 "aphorisms"), but also because these abandoned notes mainly focus on topics such as the critique of morality while touching upon the "feeling of power" only once.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eternal_return">Eternal return</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Eternal return">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eternal_return" title="Eternal return">Eternal return</a></div>
<p>"Eternal return" (also known as "eternal recurrence") is a hypothetical concept that posits that the universe has been recurring, and will continue to recur, for an infinite number of times across infinite time or space. It is a purely <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physical</a> concept, involving no supernatural <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a>, but the return of beings in the same bodies. Nietzsche first proposed the idea of eternal return in a parable in Section 341 of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gay_Science" title="The Gay Science">The Gay Science</a></i>, and also in the chapter "Of the Vision and the Riddle" in <i><a href="/wiki/Thus_Spoke_Zarathustra" title="Thus Spoke Zarathustra">Thus Spoke Zarathustra</a></i>, among other places.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENietzsche1961176â180_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENietzsche1961176â180-193">[190]</a></sup> Nietzsche considered it as potentially "horrifying and paralyzing", and said that its burden is the "heaviest weight" imaginable ("<i> das schwerste Gewicht</i>").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[191]</a></sup> The wish for the eternal return of all events would mark the ultimate affirmation of life, a reaction to <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Schopenhauer</a>'s praise of denying the will-to-live. To comprehend eternal recurrence, and to not only come to peace with it but to embrace it, requires <i><a href="/wiki/Amor_fati" title="Amor fati">amor fati</a></i>, "love of fate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dudl_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-dudl-195">[192]</a></sup> As <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heidegger" title="Heidegger">Heidegger</a> pointed out in his lectures on Nietzsche, Nietzsche's first mention of eternal recurrence presents this concept as a <a href="/wiki/Thought_experiment" title="Thought experiment">hypothetical <i>question</i></a> rather than stating it as fact. According to Heidegger, it is the burden imposed by the <i>question</i> of eternal recurrence â the mere possibility of it, and the reality of speculating on that possibility â which is so significant in modern thought: "The way Nietzsche here patterns the first communication of the thought of the 'greatest burden' [of eternal recurrence] makes it clear that this 'thought of thoughts' is at the same time 'the most burdensome thought.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Nehamas" title="Alexander Nehamas">Alexander Nehamas</a> writes in <i>Nietzsche: Life as Literature</i> of three ways of seeing the eternal recurrence:
</p>
<ol><li>"My life will recur in exactly identical fashion:" this expresses a totally <a href="/wiki/Fatalism" title="Fatalism">fatalistic</a> approach to the idea;</li>
<li>"My life may recur in exactly identical fashion:" This second view conditionally asserts <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>, but fails to capture what Nietzsche refers to in <i>The Gay Science</i>, p.Â 341; and finally,</li>
<li>"If my life were to recur, then it could recur only in identical fashion." Nehamas shows that this interpretation exists totally independently of physics and does not presuppose the truth of cosmology.</li></ol>
<p>Nehamas concluded that, if individuals constitute themselves through their actions, they can only maintain themselves in their current state by living in a recurrence of past actions (Nehamas, 153). Nietzsche's thought is the negation of the idea of a history of salvation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tongeren2000_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tongeren2000-197">[194]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id=".C3.9Cbermensch"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ãbermensch">Ãbermensch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Ãbermensch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Cbermensch" title="Ãbermensch">Ãbermensch</a></div>
<p>Another concept important to understanding Nietzsche is the <i>Ãbermensch</i> (Superman).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELampert1986_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELampert1986-200">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[198]</a></sup> Writing about nihilism in <i><a href="/wiki/Thus_Spoke_Zarathustra" title="Thus Spoke Zarathustra">Also Sprach Zarathustra</a></i>, Nietzsche introduced an <i>Ãbermensch</i>. According to <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Lampert" title="Laurence Lampert">Laurence Lampert</a>, "the death of God must be followed by a long twilight of piety and nihilism (II. 19; III. 8). Zarathustra's gift of the overman is given to mankind not aware of the problem to which the overman is the solution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELampert198618_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELampert198618-202">[199]</a></sup> Zarathustra presents the <i>Ãbermensch</i> as the creator of new values, and he appears as a solution to the problem of the death of God and nihilism. The <i>Ãbermensch</i> does not follow the morality of common people since that favors mediocrity but rises above the notion of <a href="/wiki/Good_and_evil" title="Good and evil">good and evil</a> and above the "<a href="/wiki/Herd_behavior" title="Herd behavior">herd</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[200]</a></sup> In this way Zarathustra proclaims his ultimate goal as the journey towards the state of the <i>Ãbermensch</i>. He wants a kind of spiritual evolution of self-awareness and overcoming of traditional views on morality and justice that stem from the <a href="/wiki/Superstition" title="Superstition">superstition</a> beliefs still deeply rooted or related to the notion of God and Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[201]</a></sup>
</p><p>From <i>Thus Spoke Zarathustra</i> (Zarathustra's Prologue; pp.Â 9â11):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[202]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><i>I teach you the Ãbermensch</i>. Man is something that shall be overcome. What have you done to overcome him? All beings so far have created something beyond themselves: and you want to be the ebb of that great tide, and would rather go back to the beast than overcome man? What is the ape to man? A laughing-stock or a painful embarrassment. And just the same shall man be to the Ãbermensch: a laughing-stock or a painful embarrassment. You have made your way from worm to man, and much within you is still worm. Once you were apes, and even yet man is more of an ape than any ape. Even the wisest among you is only a conflict and hybrid of plant and ghost. But do I bid you become ghosts or plants? Behold, I teach you the Ãbermensch! The Ãbermensch is the meaning of the earth. Let your will say: The Ãbermensch <i>shall be</i> the meaning of the earth... Man is a rope stretched between the animal and the Ãbermenschâa rope over an abyss... What is great in man is that he is a bridge and not a goal: what is lovable in man is that he is an <i>over-going</i> and a <i>going under</i>.</p></blockquote>
<p>Zarathustra contrasts the <i>Ãbermensch</i> with the <a href="/wiki/Last_man" title="Last man">last man</a> of egalitarian modernity (the most obvious example being democracy), an alternative goal humanity might set for itself. The last man is possible only by mankind's having bred an <a href="/wiki/Apathy" title="Apathy">apathetic</a> creature who has no great passion or commitment, who is unable to dream, who merely earns his living and keeps warm. This concept appears only in <i>Thus Spoke Zarathustra</i>, and is presented as a condition that would render the creation of the <i>Ãbermensch</i> impossible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[204]</a></sup> have suggested that the eternal return is related to the <i>Ãbermensch</i>, since willing the eternal return of the same is a necessary step if the <i>Ãbermensch</i> is to create new values untainted by the spirit of gravity or <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">asceticism</a>. Values involve a rank-ordering of things, and so are inseparable from approval and disapproval, yet it was dissatisfaction that prompted men to seek refuge in other-worldliness and embrace other-worldly values. It could seem that the <i>Ãbermensch</i>, in being devoted to any values at all, would necessarily fail to create values that did not share some bit of asceticism. Willing the eternal recurrence is presented as accepting the existence of the low while still recognizing it as the low, and thus as overcoming the spirit of gravity or asceticism. One must have the strength of the <i>Ãbermensch</i> to will the eternal recurrence. Only the <i>Ãbermensch</i> will have the strength to fully accept all of his past life, including his failures and misdeeds, and to truly will their eternal return. This action nearly kills Zarathustra, for example, and most human beings cannot avoid other-worldliness because they really are sick, not because of any choice they made.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wochenspruch_der_NSDAP_9_April_1939.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="614" data-file-width="415" decoding="async" height="325" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Wochenspruch_der_NSDAP_9_April_1939.jpg/220px-Wochenspruch_der_NSDAP_9_April_1939.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Wochenspruch_der_NSDAP_9_April_1939.jpg/330px-Wochenspruch_der_NSDAP_9_April_1939.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5a/Wochenspruch_der_NSDAP_9_April_1939.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wochenspruch_der_NSDAP_9_April_1939.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Wochenspruch_der_NSDAP" title="Wochenspruch der NSDAP">Wochenspruch der NSDAP</a></i> 9 April 1939: "What does not kill me makes me stronger."</div></div></div>
<p>The Nazis attempted to incorporate the concept into their ideology by means of taking Nietzsche's figurative form of speech and creating a literal superiority over other ethnicities. After his death, <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_F%C3%B6rster-Nietzsche" title="Elisabeth FÃ¶rster-Nietzsche">Elisabeth FÃ¶rster-Nietzsche</a> became the curator and editor of her brother's manuscripts. She reworked Nietzsche's unpublished writings to fit her own <a href="/wiki/German_nationalism#1871_to_World_War_I,_1914â1918" title="German nationalism">German nationalist</a> ideology while often contradicting or obfuscating Nietzsche's stated opinions, which were explicitly <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_Friedrich_Nietzsche#Nietzsche's_criticism_of_anti-Semitism_and_nationalism" title="Philosophy of Friedrich Nietzsche">opposed to antisemitism and nationalism</a>. Through her published editions, Nietzsche's work became associated with <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">fascism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golomb_2002_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Golomb_2002-208">[205]</a></sup> 20th-century scholars contested this interpretation of his work and corrected editions of his writings were soon made available.
</p><p>Although Nietzsche has been misrepresented as a predecessor to Nazism, he criticized anti-Semitism, <a href="/wiki/Pan-Germanism" title="Pan-Germanism">pan-Germanism</a> and, to a lesser extent, <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[206]</a></sup> Thus, he broke with his editor in 1886 because of his opposition to his editor's anti-Semitic stances, and his rupture with <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a>, expressed in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Case_of_Wagner" title="The Case of Wagner">The Case of Wagner</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Nietzsche_contra_Wagner" title="Nietzsche contra Wagner">Nietzsche contra Wagner</a>,</i> both of which he wrote in 1888, had much to do with Wagner's endorsement of pan-Germanism and anti-Semitismâand also of his rallying to Christianity. In a 29 March 1887 letter to <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Fritsch" title="Theodor Fritsch">Theodor Fritsch</a>, Nietzsche mocked anti-Semites, Fritsch, <a href="/wiki/Eugen_D%C3%BChring" title="Eugen DÃ¼hring">Eugen DÃ¼hring</a>, Wagner, Ebrard, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Wahrmund" title="Adolf Wahrmund">Wahrmund</a>, and the leading advocate of pan-Germanism, <a href="/wiki/Paul_de_Lagarde" title="Paul de Lagarde">Paul de Lagarde</a>, who would become, along with Wagner and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Houston_Chamberlain" title="Houston Chamberlain">Houston Chamberlain</a>, the main official influences of <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bataille_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bataille-79">[78]</a></sup> This 1887 letter to Fritsch ended by: "And finally, how do you think I feel when the name Zarathustra is mouthed by anti-Semites?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[207]</a></sup> In contrast to these examples, Nietzsche's close friend <a href="/wiki/Franz_Overbeck" title="Franz Overbeck">Franz Overbeck</a> recalled in his memoirs, "When he
speaks frankly, the opinions he expresses about Jews go, in their severity, beyond any anti-Semitism. The foundation of his anti-Christianity is essentially anti-Semitic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[208]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Critique_of_mass_culture">Critique of mass culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Critique of mass culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Friedrich Nietzsche held a pessimistic view of modern society and culture. He believed the press and mass culture led to conformity, brought about mediocrity, and the lack of intellectual progress was leading to the decline of the human species. In his opinion, some people would be able to become superior individuals through the use of willpower. By rising above mass culture, those persons would produce higher, brighter, and healthier human beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[209]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reading_and_influence">Reading and influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Reading and influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche_Archives_in_Weimar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Nietzsche_Archives_in_Weimar.jpg/220px-Nietzsche_Archives_in_Weimar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Nietzsche_Archives_in_Weimar.jpg/330px-Nietzsche_Archives_in_Weimar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Nietzsche_Archives_in_Weimar.jpg/440px-Nietzsche_Archives_in_Weimar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche_Archives_in_Weimar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The residence of Nietzsche's last three years along with archive in <a href="/wiki/Weimar" title="Weimar">Weimar</a>, Germany, which holds many of Nietzsche's papers</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Library of Friedrich Nietzsche">Library of Friedrich Nietzsche</a></div>
<p>A trained philologist, Nietzsche had a thorough knowledge of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" title="Greek philosophy">Greek philosophy</a>. He read <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Kant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">Mill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Schopenhauer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afrikan_Spir" title="Afrikan Spir">Spir</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[210]</a></sup> who became the main opponents in his philosophy, and later engaged, via the work of <a href="/wiki/Kuno_Fischer" title="Kuno Fischer">Kuno Fischer</a> in particular, with the thought of <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a>, whom he saw as his "precursor" in many respects<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[212]</a></sup> but as a personification of the "ascetic ideal" in others. However, Nietzsche referred to Kant as a "moral fanatic", Plato as "boring", Mill as a "blockhead", and of Spinoza, he asked: "How much of personal timidity and vulnerability does this masquerade of a sickly recluse betray?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell2004693â697_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell2004693â697-216">[213]</a></sup> He likewise expressed contempt for British author <a href="/wiki/George_Eliot" title="George Eliot">George Eliot</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nietzsche's philosophy, while innovative and revolutionary, was indebted to many predecessors. While at Basel, Nietzsche lectured on pre-Platonic philosophers for several years, and the text of this lecture series has been characterized as a "lost link" in the development of his thought. "In it, concepts such as the will to power, the eternal return of the same, the overman, gay science, self-overcoming and so on receive rough, unnamed formulations and are linked to specific pre-Platonic, especially Heraclitus, who emerges as a pre-Platonic Nietzsche."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENietzsche2001xxxvii_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENietzsche2001xxxvii-218">[215]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Socratic" title="Pre-Socratic">pre-Socratic</a> thinker <a href="/wiki/Heraclitus" title="Heraclitus">Heraclitus</a> was known for rejecting the concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Being" title="Being">being</a> as a constant and eternal principle of the universe and embracing "flux" and incessant change. His symbolism of the world as "child play" marked by amoral spontaneity and lack of definite rules was appreciated by Nietzsche.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoochnik200437â39_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoochnik200437â39-219">[216]</a></sup> Due to his Heraclitean sympathies, Nietzsche was also a vociferous critic of <a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a>, who, in contrast to Heraclitus, viewed the world as a single, unchanging Being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoochnik200448_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoochnik200448-220">[217]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his <i>Egotism in German Philosophy</i>, <a href="/wiki/George_Santayana" title="George Santayana">George Santayana</a> claimed that Nietzsche's whole philosophy was a reaction to Schopenhauer. Santayana wrote that Nietzsche's work was "an emendation of that of Schopenhauer. The will to live would become the will to dominate; pessimism founded on reflection would become optimism founded on courage; the suspense of the will in contemplation would yield to a more biological account of intelligence and taste; finally in the place of pity and asceticism (Schopenhauer's two principles of morals) Nietzsche would set up the duty of asserting the will at all costs and being cruelly but beautifully strong. These points of difference from Schopenhauer cover the whole philosophy of Nietzsche."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESantayana1916114_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESantayana1916114-221">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>The superficial similarity of Nietzsche's <i>Ãbermensch</i> to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Carlyle" title="Thomas Carlyle">Thomas Carlyle</a>'s Hero as well as both authors' rhetorical prose style has led to speculation concerning the degree to which Nietzsche might have been influenced by his reading of Carlyle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[223]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/G._K._Chesterton" title="G. K. Chesterton">G. K. Chesterton</a> believed that "Out of [Carlyle] flows most of the philosophy of Nietzsche", qualifying his statement by adding that they were "profoundly different" in character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[224]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ruth_apRoberts" title="Ruth apRoberts">Ruth apRoberts</a> has shown that Carlyle anticipated Nietzsche in asserting the importance of metaphor (with Nietzsche's metaphor-fiction theory "appear[ing] to owe something to Carlyle"), announcing the death of God, and recognizing both Goethe's <i>Entsagen</i> (renunciation) and <a href="/wiki/Novalis" title="Novalis">Novalis</a>'s <i>SelbsttÃ¶dtung</i> (self-annihilation) as prerequisites for engaging in philosophy. apRoberts writes that "Nietzsche and Carlyle had the same German sources, but Nietzsche may owe more to Carlyle than he cares to admit", noting that "[Nietzsche] takes the trouble to repudiate Carlyle with malicious emphasis."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[225]</a></sup> Ralph Jessop, <a href="/wiki/Senior_lecturer" title="Senior lecturer">senior lecturer</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Glasgow" title="University of Glasgow">University of Glasgow</a>, has recently argued that a reassessment of Carlyle's influence on Nietzsche is "long-overdue".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[226]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nietzsche expressed admiration for 17th-century French moralists such as <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_de_La_Rochefoucauld_(writer)" title="FranÃ§ois de La Rochefoucauld (writer)">La Rochefoucauld</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean_de_La_Bruy%C3%A8re" title="Jean de La BruyÃ¨re">La BruyÃ¨re</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luc_de_Clapiers,_marquis_de_Vauvenargues" title="Luc de Clapiers, marquis de Vauvenargues">Vauvenargues</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[227]</a></sup> as well as for <a href="/wiki/Stendhal" title="Stendhal">Stendhal</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTENietzsche1888d"Why_I_am_So_Clever",_Â§&amp;nbsp;3_231-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTENietzsche1888d"Why_I_am_So_Clever",_Â§&amp;nbsp;3-231'>[228]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Organicism" title="Organicism">organicism</a> of <a href="/wiki/Paul_Bourget" title="Paul Bourget">Paul Bourget</a> influenced Nietzsche,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[229]</a></sup> as did that of <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Virchow" title="Rudolf Virchow">Rudolf Virchow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Espinas" title="Alfred Espinas">Alfred Espinas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[230]</a></sup> In 1867 Nietzsche wrote in a letter that he was trying to improve his German style of writing with the help of <a href="/wiki/Gotthold_Ephraim_Lessing" title="Gotthold Ephraim Lessing">Lessing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georg_Christoph_Lichtenberg" title="Georg Christoph Lichtenberg">Lichtenberg</a> and Schopenhauer. It was probably Lichtenberg (along with <a href="/wiki/Paul_R%C3%A9e" title="Paul RÃ©e">Paul RÃ©e</a>) whose aphoristic style of writing contributed to Nietzsche's own use of <a href="/wiki/Aphorism" title="Aphorism">aphorism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[231]</a></sup> Nietzsche early learned of <a href="/wiki/Darwinism" title="Darwinism">Darwinism</a> through <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Albert_Lange" title="Friedrich Albert Lange">Friedrich Albert Lange</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fouillee_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fouillee-235">[232]</a></sup> The essays of <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson">Ralph Waldo Emerson</a> had a profound influence on Nietzsche, who "loved Emerson from first to last",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[233]</a></sup> wrote "Never have I felt so much at home in a book", and called him "[the] author who has been richest in ideas in this century so far".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[234]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hippolyte_Taine" title="Hippolyte Taine">Hippolyte Taine</a> influenced Nietzsche's view on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rousseau" title="Rousseau">Rousseau</a> and <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[235]</a></sup> Notably, he also read some of the posthumous works of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Baudelaire" title="Charles Baudelaire">Charles Baudelaire</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mazzino_Montinari_1996_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mazzino_Montinari_1996-239">[236]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Tolstoy</a>'s <i>My Religion</i>, <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Renan" title="Ernest Renan">Ernest Renan</a>'s <i>Life of Jesus</i>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky">Fyodor Dostoyevsky</a>'s <i>Demons</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mazzino_Montinari_1996_239-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mazzino_Montinari_1996-239">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaufmann1974306â340_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaufmann1974306â340-240">[237]</a></sup> Nietzsche called Dostoyevsky "the only psychologist from whom I have anything to learn".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENietzsche1888bÂ§&amp;nbsp;45_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENietzsche1888bÂ§&amp;nbsp;45-241">[238]</a></sup> While Nietzsche
