never mentions <a href="/wiki/Max_Stirner" title="Max Stirner">Max Stirner</a>, the similarities in their ideas have prompted a minority of interpreters to suggest a <a href="/wiki/Relationship_between_Friedrich_Nietzsche_and_Max_Stirner" title="Relationship between Friedrich Nietzsche and Max Stirner">relationship between the two</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[239]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[241]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[243]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[245]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1861 Nietzsche wrote an enthusiastic essay on his "favorite poet," <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_H%C3%B6lderlin" title="Friedrich HÃ¶lderlin">Friedrich HÃ¶lderlin</a>, mostly forgotten at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[246]</a></sup> He also expressed deep appreciation for Stifter's <i><a href="/wiki/Der_Nachsommer" title="Der Nachsommer">Indian Summer</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meyer-Sickendiek,_Burkhard_2004._p._323_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meyer-Sickendiek,_Burkhard_2004._p._323-250">[247]</a></sup> Byron's <i><a href="/wiki/Manfred" title="Manfred">Manfred</a></i> and Twain's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Tom_Sawyer" title="The Adventures of Tom Sawyer">Tom Sawyer</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[248]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception_and_legacy">Reception and legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Reception and legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Influence_and_reception_of_Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Influence and reception of Friedrich Nietzsche">Influence and reception of Friedrich Nietzsche</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Friederich_Nietzsche.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="953" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Friederich_Nietzsche.jpg/170px-Friederich_Nietzsche.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Friederich_Nietzsche.jpg/255px-Friederich_Nietzsche.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Friederich_Nietzsche.jpg/340px-Friederich_Nietzsche.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Friederich_Nietzsche.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Nietzsche by <a href="/wiki/Edvard_Munch" title="Edvard Munch">Edvard Munch</a>, 1906</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche-Denkmal_Naumburg_2013.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2183" data-file-width="1917" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nietzsche-Denkmal_Naumburg_2013.jpg/170px-Nietzsche-Denkmal_Naumburg_2013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nietzsche-Denkmal_Naumburg_2013.jpg/255px-Nietzsche-Denkmal_Naumburg_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nietzsche-Denkmal_Naumburg_2013.jpg/340px-Nietzsche-Denkmal_Naumburg_2013.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche-Denkmal_Naumburg_2013.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Nietzsche in Naumburg</div></div></div>
<p>Nietzsche's works did not reach a wide readership during his active writing career. However, in 1888 the influential Danish critic <a href="/wiki/Georg_Brandes" title="Georg Brandes">Georg Brandes</a> aroused considerable excitement about Nietzsche through a series of lectures he gave at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Copenhagen" title="University of Copenhagen">University of Copenhagen</a>. In the years after Nietzsche's death in 1900, his works became better known, and readers have responded to them in complex and sometimes controversial ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[249]</a></sup> Many Germans eventually discovered his appeals for greater <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individualism</a> and personality development in <i><a href="/wiki/Thus_Spoke_Zarathustra" title="Thus Spoke Zarathustra">Thus Spoke Zarathustra</a></i>, but responded to them divergently. He had some following among left-wing Germans in the 1890s; in 1894â1895 German conservatives wanted to ban his work as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subversive" title="Subversive">subversive</a>. During the late 19th century <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_and_Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Anarchism and Friedrich Nietzsche">Nietzsche's ideas were commonly associated with anarchist movements</a> and appear to have had influence within them, particularly in France and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[252]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H.L._Mencken" title="H.L. Mencken">H.L. Mencken</a> produced the first book on Nietzsche in English in 1907, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Philosophy_of_Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="The Philosophy of Friedrich Nietzsche">The Philosophy of Friedrich Nietzsche</a></i>, and in 1910 a book of translated paragraphs from Nietzsche, increasing knowledge of his philosophy in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[253]</a></sup> Nietzsche is known today as a precursor to <a href="/wiki/Existentialism" title="Existentialism">existentialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Post-structuralism" title="Post-structuralism">post-structuralism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Postmodern_philosophy" title="Postmodern philosophy">postmodernism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[254]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/W._B._Yeats" title="W. B. Yeats">W. B. Yeats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Symons" title="Arthur Symons">Arthur Symons</a> described Nietzsche as the intellectual heir to <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">William Blake</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[255]</a></sup> Symons went on to compare the ideas of the two thinkers in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Symbolist_Movement_in_Literature" title="The Symbolist Movement in Literature">The Symbolist Movement in Literature</a></i>, while Yeats tried to raise awareness of Nietzsche in Ireland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[256]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[257]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[258]</a></sup> A similar notion was espoused by <a href="/wiki/W._H._Auden" title="W. H. Auden">W. H. Auden</a> who wrote of Nietzsche in his <i>New Year Letter</i> (released in 1941 in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Double_Man_(book)" title="The Double Man (book)">The Double Man</a></i>): "O masterly <a href="/wiki/Debunker" title="Debunker">debunker</a> of our liberal fallacies ... all your life you stormed, like your English forerunner Blake."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[260]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[261]</a></sup> Nietzsche made an impact on composers during the 1890s. Writer <a href="/wiki/Donald_Mitchell_(writer)" title="Donald Mitchell (writer)">Donald Mitchell</a> noted that <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Mahler" title="Gustav Mahler">Gustav Mahler</a> was "attracted to the poetic fire of Zarathustra, but repelled by the intellectual core of its writings". He also quoted Mahler himself, and adds that he was influenced by Nietzsche's conception and affirmative approach to nature, which Mahler presented in his <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._3_(Mahler)" title="Symphony No. 3 (Mahler)">Third Symphony</a> using <a href="/wiki/Zarathustra%27s_roundelay" title="Zarathustra's roundelay">Zarathustra's roundelay</a>. <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Delius" title="Frederick Delius">Frederick Delius</a> produced a piece of choral music, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Mass_of_Life" title="A Mass of Life">A Mass of Life</a></i>, based on a text of <i>Thus Spoke Zarathustra</i>, while <a href="/wiki/Richard_Strauss" title="Richard Strauss">Richard Strauss</a> (who also based his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Also_sprach_Zarathustra_(Strauss)" title="Also sprach Zarathustra (Strauss)"><i>Also sprach Zarathustra</i></a> on the same book), was only interested in finishing "another chapter of symphonic autobiography".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[262]</a></sup> Writers and poets influenced by Nietzsche include <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Gide" title="AndrÃ© Gide">AndrÃ© Gide</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[263]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/August_Strindberg" title="August Strindberg">August Strindberg</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[264]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robinson_Jeffers" title="Robinson Jeffers">Robinson Jeffers</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[265]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/P%C3%ADo_Baroja" title="PÃ­o Baroja">PÃ­o Baroja</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[266]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D.H._Lawrence" title="D.H. Lawrence">D.H. Lawrence</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[267]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edith_S%C3%B6dergran" title="Edith SÃ¶dergran">Edith SÃ¶dergran</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[268]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Yukio_Mishima" title="Yukio Mishima">Yukio Mishima</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[269]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nietzsche was an early influence on the poetry of <a href="/wiki/Rainer_Maria_Rilke" title="Rainer Maria Rilke">Rainer Maria Rilke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[270]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Knut_Hamsun" title="Knut Hamsun">Knut Hamsun</a> counted Nietzsche, along with <a href="/wiki/August_Strindberg" title="August Strindberg">Strindberg</a> and Dostoyevsky, as his primary influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[271]</a></sup> Author <a href="/wiki/Jack_London" title="Jack London">Jack London</a> wrote that he was more stimulated by Nietzsche than by any other writer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[272]</a></sup> Critics have suggested that the character of David Grief in <i><a href="/wiki/A_Son_of_the_Sun_(novel)" title="A Son of the Sun (novel)">A Son of the Sun</a></i> was based on Nietzsche.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[273]</a></sup> Nietzsche's influence on <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Iqbal" title="Muhammad Iqbal">Muhammad Iqbal</a> is most evidenced in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secrets_of_the_Self" title="The Secrets of the Self">Asrar-i-Khudi</a></i> (<i>The Secrets of the Self</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[274]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wallace_Stevens" title="Wallace Stevens">Wallace Stevens</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[275]</a></sup> was another reader of Nietzsche, and elements of Nietzsche's philosophy were found throughout Stevens's poetry collection <i><a href="/wiki/Harmonium_(poetry_collection)" title="Harmonium (poetry collection)">Harmonium</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[276]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[277]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Olaf_Stapledon" title="Olaf Stapledon">Olaf Stapledon</a> was influenced by the idea of the <i>Ãbermensch</i> and it is a central theme in his books <i><a href="/wiki/Odd_John" title="Odd John">Odd John</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sirius_(novel)" title="Sirius (novel)">Sirius</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[278]</a></sup> In Russia, Nietzsche influenced <a href="/wiki/Russian_symbolism" title="Russian symbolism">Russian symbolism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[279]</a></sup> and figures such as <a href="/wiki/Dmitry_Merezhkovsky" title="Dmitry Merezhkovsky">Dmitry Merezhkovsky</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[280]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Andrei_Bely" title="Andrei Bely">Andrei Bely</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[281]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vyacheslav_Ivanov_(poet)" title="Vyacheslav Ivanov (poet)">Vyacheslav Ivanov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Scriabin" title="Alexander Scriabin">Alexander Scriabin</a> incorporated or discussed parts of Nietzsche philosophy in their works. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Mann" title="Thomas Mann">Thomas Mann</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Death_in_Venice" title="Death in Venice">Death in Venice</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[282]</a></sup> shows a use of Apollonian and Dionysian, and in <i><a href="/wiki/Doctor_Faustus_(novel)" title="Doctor Faustus (novel)">Doctor Faustus</a></i> Nietzsche was a central source for the character of Adrian LeverkÃ¼hn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[283]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[284]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Hesse" title="Hermann Hesse">Hermann Hesse</a>, similarly, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_and_Goldmund" title="Narcissus and Goldmund">Narcissus and Goldmund</a></i> presents two main characters as opposite yet intertwined Apollonian and Dionysian spirits. Painter <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Segantini" title="Giovanni Segantini">Giovanni Segantini</a> was fascinated by <i>Thus Spoke Zarathustra</i>, and he drew an illustration for the first Italian translation of the book. The Russian painter <a href="/wiki/Lena_Hades" title="Lena Hades">Lena Hades</a> created the oil painting cycle <i><a href="/wiki/Also_Sprach_Zarathustra_(painting)" title="Also Sprach Zarathustra (painting)">Also Sprach Zarathustra</a></i> dedicated to the book <i>Thus Spoke Zarathustra</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[285]</a></sup>
</p><p>By <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, Nietzsche had acquired a reputation as an inspiration for right-wing German <a href="/wiki/Militarism" title="Militarism">militarism</a> and leftist politics. German soldiers received copies of <i>Thus Spoke Zarathustra</i> as gifts during World War I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[286]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaufmann19748_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaufmann19748-290">[287]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dreyfus_affair" title="Dreyfus affair">Dreyfus affair</a> provided a contrasting example of his reception: the French antisemitic Right labelled the Jewish and leftist intellectuals who defended <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Dreyfus" title="Alfred Dreyfus">Alfred Dreyfus</a> as "Nietzscheans".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[288]</a></sup> Nietzsche had a distinct appeal for many <a href="/wiki/Zionism" title="Zionism">Zionist</a> thinkers around the start of the 20th century, most notable being <a href="/wiki/Ahad_Ha%27am" title="Ahad Ha'am">Ahad Ha'am</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[289]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hillel_Zeitlin" title="Hillel Zeitlin">Hillel Zeitlin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[290]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Micha_Josef_Berdyczewski" title="Micha Josef Berdyczewski">Micha Josef Berdyczewski</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.D._Gordon" title="A.D. Gordon">A.D. Gordon</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[291]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Martin_Buber" title="Martin Buber">Martin Buber</a>, who went so far as to extoll Nietzsche as a "creator" and "emissary of life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGolomb1997234â235_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGolomb1997234â235-295">[292]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chaim_Weizmann" title="Chaim Weizmann">Chaim Weizmann</a> was a great admirer of Nietzsche; the first president of Israel sent Nietzsche's books to his wife, adding a comment in a letter that "This was the best and finest thing I can send to you."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[293]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Israel_Eldad" title="Israel Eldad">Israel Eldad</a>, the ideological chief of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stern_Gang" title="Stern Gang">Stern Gang</a> that fought the British in <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">Palestine</a> in the 1940s, wrote about Nietzsche in his underground newspaper and later translated most of Nietzsche's books into <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[294]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eugene_O%27Neill" title="Eugene O'Neill">Eugene O'Neill</a> remarked that <i>Zarathustra</i> influenced him more than any other book he ever read. He also shared Nietzsche's view of <a href="/wiki/Tragedy" title="Tragedy">tragedy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[295]</a></sup> The plays <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_God_Brown" title="The Great God Brown">The Great God Brown</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Lazarus_Laughed" title="Lazarus Laughed">Lazarus Laughed</a></i> are examples of Nietzsche's influence on him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[296]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[297]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[298]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Workingmen%27s_Association" title="International Workingmen's Association">First International</a> claimed Nietzsche as ideologically one of their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[299]</a></sup> From 1888 through the 1890s there were more publications of Nietzsche works in Russia than in any other country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[300]</a></sup> Nietzsche was influential among the Bolsheviks. Among the Nietzschean Bolsheviks were <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Bazarov" title="Vladimir Bazarov">Vladimir Bazarov</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[301]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anatoly_Lunacharsky" title="Anatoly Lunacharsky">Anatoly Lunacharsky</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[302]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aleksandr_Bogdanov" title="Aleksandr Bogdanov">Aleksandr Bogdanov</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[303]</a></sup> Nietzsche's influence on the works of <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_School" title="Frankfurt School">Frankfurt School</a> philosophers <a href="/wiki/Max_Horkheimer" title="Max Horkheimer">Max Horkheimer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theodor_W._Adorno" title="Theodor W. Adorno">Theodor W. Adorno</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[304]</a></sup> can be seen in the <i><a href="/wiki/Dialectic_of_Enlightenment" title="Dialectic of Enlightenment">Dialectic of Enlightenment</a></i>. Adorno summed up Nietzsche's philosophy as expressing the "humane in a world in which humanity has become a sham".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[305]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nietzsche's growing prominence suffered a severe setback when his works became closely associated with <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>. Many political leaders of the twentieth century were at least superficially familiar with Nietzsche's ideas, although it is not always possible to determine whether they actually read his work. It is debated among scholars whether Hitler read Nietzsche, although if he did, it may not have been extensively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[iv]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[v]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[306]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[307]</a></sup> He was a frequent visitor to the Nietzsche museum in Weimar and used expressions of Nietzsche's, such as "lords of the earth" in <i><a href="/wiki/Mein_Kampf" title="Mein Kampf">Mein Kampf</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[308]</a></sup> The Nazis made selective use of Nietzsche's philosophy. <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Baeumler" title="Alfred Baeumler">Alfred Baeumler</a> was perhaps the most notable exponent of Nietzschean thought in Nazi Germany. Baeumler had published his book "Nietzsche, Philosopher and Politician" in 1931, before the Nazis' rise to power, and subsequently published several editions of Nietzsche's work during the Third Reich.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[309]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[310]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Mussolini</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[311]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[312]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[313]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Huey_P._Newton" title="Huey P. Newton">Huey P. Newton</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[314]</a></sup> read Nietzsche. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> read Nietzsche with "curious interest", and his book <i>Beyond Peace</i> might have taken its title from Nietzsche's book <i>Beyond Good and Evil</i> which Nixon read beforehand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[315]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a> wrote that Nietzsche had exerted great influence on philosophers and on people of literary and artistic culture, but warned that the attempt to put Nietzsche's philosophy of aristocracy into practice could only be done by an organization similar to the Fascist or the Nazi party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell_1945_766_&amp;_770_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russell_1945_766_&amp;_770-321">[316]</a></sup>
</p><p>A decade after World War II, there was a revival of Nietzsche's philosophical writings thanks to translations and analyses by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Kaufmann_(philosopher)" title="Walter Kaufmann (philosopher)">Walter Kaufmann</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R.J._Hollingdale" title="R.J. Hollingdale">R.J. Hollingdale</a>. <a href="/wiki/Georges_Bataille" title="Georges Bataille">Georges Bataille</a> was also influential in this revival, defending Nietzsche against appropriation by the Nazis with his notable 1937 essay "Nietzsche and Fascists".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[317]</a></sup> Others, well known philosophers in their own right, wrote commentaries on Nietzsche's philosophy, including <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heidegger" title="Martin Heidegger">Martin Heidegger</a>, who produced a four-volume study, and <a href="/wiki/Lev_Shestov" title="Lev Shestov">Lev Shestov</a>, who wrote a book called <i>Dostoyevski, Tolstoy and Nietzsche</i> where he portrays Nietzsche and Dostoyevski as the "thinkers of tragedy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[318]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Georg_Simmel" title="Georg Simmel">Georg Simmel</a> compares Nietzsche's importance to ethics to that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copernicus" title="Copernicus">Copernicus</a> for <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[319]</a></sup> Sociologist <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_T%C3%B6nnies" title="Ferdinand TÃ¶nnies">Ferdinand TÃ¶nnies</a> read Nietzsche avidly from his early life, and later frequently discussed many of his concepts in his own works. Nietzsche has influenced philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heidegger" title="Martin Heidegger">Martin Heidegger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Paul_Sartre" title="Jean-Paul Sartre">Jean-Paul Sartre</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[320]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Spengler" title="Oswald Spengler">Oswald Spengler</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[321]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_Grant_(philosopher)" title="George Grant (philosopher)">George Grant</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[322]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Emil_Cioran" title="Emil Cioran">Emil Cioran</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[323]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Albert_Camus" title="Albert Camus">Albert Camus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ayn_Rand" title="Ayn Rand">Ayn Rand</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[324]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Derrida" title="Jacques Derrida">Jacques Derrida</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[325]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Kofman" title="Sarah Kofman">Sarah Kofman</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[326]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Leo_Strauss" title="Leo Strauss">Leo Strauss</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[327]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Max_Scheler" title="Max Scheler">Max Scheler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michel_Foucault" title="Michel Foucault">Michel Foucault</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[328]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Williams" title="Bernard Williams">Bernard Williams</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[329]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Nick_Land" title="Nick Land">Nick Land</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[330]</a></sup>
</p><p>Camus described Nietzsche as "the only artist to have derived the extreme consequences of an aesthetics of the <a href="/wiki/Absurdism" title="Absurdism">absurd</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[331]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ric%C5%93ur" title="Paul RicÅur">Paul RicÅur</a> called Nietzsche one of the masters of the "school of suspicion", alongside <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> and Sigmund Freud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RicÅur_337-0"><a href="#cite_note-RicÅur-337">[332]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Jung" title="Carl Jung">Carl Jung</a> was also influenced by Nietzsche.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[333]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Memories,_Dreams,_Reflections" title="Memories, Dreams, Reflections">Memories, Dreams, Reflections</a></i>, a biography transcribed by his secretary, he cites Nietzsche as a large influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[334]</a></sup> Aspects of Nietzsche's philosophy, especially his ideas of the self and his relation to society, run through much of late-twentieth and early twenty-first century thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belliotti_340-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belliotti-340">[335]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[336]</a></sup> Nietzsche's writings have also been influential to some advancers of <a href="/wiki/Accelerationism" title="Accelerationism">Accelerationist</a> thought through his influence on <a href="/wiki/Deleuze_and_Guattari" title="Deleuze and Guattari">Deleuze and Guattari</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[337]</a></sup> His deepening of the romantic-heroic tradition of the nineteenth century, for example, as expressed in the ideal of the "grand striver" appears in the work of thinkers from <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_Castoriadis" title="Cornelius Castoriadis">Cornelius Castoriadis</a> to <a href="/wiki/Roberto_Mangabeira_Unger" title="Roberto Mangabeira Unger">Roberto Mangabeira Unger</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[338]</a></sup> For Nietzsche, this grand striver overcomes obstacles, engages in epic struggles, pursues new goals, embraces recurrent novelty, and transcends existing structures and contexts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belliotti_340-1"><a href="#cite_note-Belliotti-340">[335]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 195">:â195â</span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche-Stein_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2472" data-file-width="3296" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Nietzsche-Stein_01.jpg/220px-Nietzsche-Stein_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Nietzsche-Stein_01.jpg/330px-Nietzsche-Stein_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Nietzsche-Stein_01.jpg/440px-Nietzsche-Stein_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche-Stein_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i>Nietzsche Stone</i>, near <a href="/wiki/Surlej" title="Surlej">Surlej</a>, the inspiration for <i><a href="/wiki/Thus_Spoke_Zarathustra" title="Thus Spoke Zarathustra">Thus Spoke Zarathustra</a></i></div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1093669538">.mw-parser-output .portalbox{padding:0}.mw-parser-output .portalborder{border:solid #aaa 1px}.mw-parser-output .portalbox.tleft{margin:0.5em 1em 0.5em 0}.mw-parser-output .portalbox.tright{margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul{display:table;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:175px;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .portalborder>ul{padding:0.1em;background:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul>li{display:table-row}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul>li>span:first-child{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul>li>span:last-child{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle}</style><div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint plainlist portalbox portalborder tright" role="navigation">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Nietzsche&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width reflist-lower-roman" style="column-width: 35em;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Between 1868 and 1870, he published two other studies on Diogenes Laertius: <i>On the Sources of Diogenes Laertius</i> (<i>De Fontibus Diogenis Laertii</i>) Part I (1868) &amp; Part II (1869); and <i>Analecta Laertiana</i> (1870). See <a href="#CITEREFJensenHeit2014">Jensen &amp; Heit 2014</a>, p.Â 115</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-117"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-117">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This is how R. B. Pippin describes Nietzsche's views in <i>The Persistence of Subjectivity</i> (2005), p. 326.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-188"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-188">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Nietzsche comments in many notes about the matter being a hypothesis drawn from the metaphysics of substance.

Whitlock, G. 1996. "Roger Boscovich, Benedict de Spinoza and Friedrich Nietzsche: The Untold Story." <i>Nietzsche-Studien</i> 25. p. 207.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-309"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-309">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Hugh_Trevor-Roper" title="Hugh Trevor-Roper">Trevor-Roper, Hugh</a>. [1972] 2008. "Introductory essay for 'Hitler's Table Talk 1941â1944 Secret Conversations'." In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mind_of_Adolf_Hitler" title="The Mind of Adolf Hitler">The Mind of Adolf Hitler</a></i>. Enigma Books. p. xxxvii:

"We know, from his [Hitler's] secretary, that he could quote Schopenhauer by the page, and the other German philosopher of willpower, Nietzsche, whose works he afterward presented to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mussolini" title="Mussolini">Mussolini</a>, was often on his lips."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-310"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-310">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Kershaw, Ian Hitler: <i>Hubris 1889â1936</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W._W._Norton" title="W. W. Norton">W. W. Norton</a>. p. 240:

"'Landsberg,' Hitler told <a href="/wiki/Hans_Frank" title="Hans Frank">Hans Frank</a>, was his 'university paid for by the state.' He read, he said, everything he could get hold of: Nietzsche, <a href="/wiki/Houston_Stewart_Chamberlain" title="Houston Stewart Chamberlain">Houston Stewart Chamberlain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leopold_von_Ranke" title="Leopold von Ranke">Ranke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_von_Treitschke" title="Heinrich von Treitschke">Treitschke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Marx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Bismarck</a>'s Thoughts and Memories, and the war memoirs of German and allied generals and statesmen.... But Hitler's reading and reflection were anything but academic, doubtless, he did read much. However, as was noted in an earlier chapter, he made clear in <a href="/wiki/Mein_Kampf" title="Mein Kampf">My Struggle</a> that reading for him had purely an instrumental purpose. He read not for knowledge or enlightenment, but for confirmation of his own preconceptions."</span>
</li>
</ol></div>

<li id="cite_note-105"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-105">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFWarberg">Warberg, Ulla-Karin. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.today/20170624204834/http://auktionsverket.com/news/nietzsches-ring/" rel="nofollow">"Nietzsche's ring"</a>. <i>auktionsverket.com</i>. <a href="/wiki/%C3%96stermalm" title="Ãstermalm">Ãstermalm</a>, Stockholm, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>: <a href="/wiki/Stockholms_Auktionsverk" title="Stockholms Auktionsverk">Stockholms Auktionsverk</a>. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">16 August</span> 2018</span>. <q><i>In 1905, the Polish writer Bernhard Scharlitt in the spirit of Polish patriotism wrote an article about the Nietzsche family. In Herbarz Polski, a genealogy of Polish nobility, he had come across a note about a family named 'Nicki,' who could be traced back to Radwan. A member of this family named Gotard Nietzsche had left Poland for Prussia, and his descendants had eventually settled in Saxony around the year 1700.</i></q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=auktionsverket.com&amp;rft.atitle=Nietzsche%27s+ring&amp;rft.aulast=Warberg&amp;rft.aufirst=Ulla-Karin&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fauktionsverket.com%2Fnews%2Fnietzsches-ring%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFriedrich+Nietzsche"></span></span>
</li>

"Unger, Castoriadis, and the Romance of a National Future." <i><a href="/wiki/Northwestern_University_Law_Review" title="Northwestern University Law Review">Northwestern University Law Review</a></i> 82: 39.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
